With this release, we are rolling out an updated underlying architecture for Kibana itself resulting in a faster overall experience as well as productivity enhancements for the Kibana developer community. Kibana 7.9 also brings with it new dashboarding capabilities to help drive discovery and insight, Kibana Lens enhancements designed to supercharge your data visualization efforts, and a host of new mapping, and machine learning capabilities.\n</p><p>Want to experience all of these new Kibana features right now? Starting a free trial of Elasticsearch and Kibana on <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/products/elasticsearch/service\">Elastic Cloud</a> takes less than 3 minutes and is the only managed Elasticsearch offering to include these new capabilities. You can also easily <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/start\">download the latest versions of Kibana and the Elastic Stack</a>.\n</p><p>For a full list of bug fixes and other changes, don’t miss the <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/kibana/current/release-notes.html\">Kibana 7.9 release notes</a>.\n</p> <strong><h2>A new architecture for Kibana brings you snappier page loads and so much more</h2></strong><p>If the 7.9 Kibana experience feels faster, it’s not your imagination. We have been working for more than 18 months to re-think Kibana’s entire architecture from first principles in order to build a new system designed for scalability and efficiency. We are thrilled in 7.9 to report that work has been completed and that all of Kibana has been moved to this new architecture.&#160;&#160;\n</p><p>The most immediate difference you’ll notice with this migration is simply how fast Kibana pages load. Whether you are diagnosing a site outage, investigating suspicious user activity, or putting together a dashboard in time for a meeting, moving between interfaces is immediate and helps keep you in the flow of finishing your task. For the Kibana development community, this new architecture brings a number of powerful enhancements designed to help you build features faster and with greater efficiency.&#160;For more details on this&#160;effort, check out Elastic principal software engineer Josh Dover’s blog post, “<a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/blog/introducing-a-new-architecture-for-kibana\">Introducing a new architecture for Kibana</a>.”\n</p> <strong><h2>Rolling out the welcome mat for Elastic Enterprise Search in Kibana</h2></strong><p>At Elastic we talk a lot about how Kibana is the window into the <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/elastic-stack\">Elastic Stack</a> and how that stack supports Elastic’s three solutions:&#160;<a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/observability\">Elastic Observability</a>, <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/security\">Elastic Security</a>, and <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/enterprise-search\">Elastic Enterprise Search</a>. In 7.8 we reorganized the Kibana side navigation to help make observability and security applications easier to find and in 7.9 we’re completing the solution trifecta by adding enterprise search to the fold. For existing users of <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/app-search\">App Search</a> and <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/workplace-search\">Workplace Search</a>, you’ll now be able to quickly see your existing engines from within Kibana as well as launch directly into those environments. Not already using Elastic Enterprise Search, but curious to learn more? <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/blog/whats-new-elastic-enterprise-search-7-9-0-free-workplace-search-kibana-access\">Check out the What's New in Elastic Enterprise 7.9 release blog</a> to get all the greatest updates and learn more about how to bring modern, powerful search experiences to your company’s internal content, website, or mobile application.\n</p><div class=\"embed-responsive embed-responsive-16by9 shadow m-t-40 m-b-40\"><div class=\"vsc-controller\" data-vscid=\"26csir2x4\"></div><video autoplay=\"autoplay\" playsinline=\"playsinline\" muted=\"muted\" loop=\"loop\" data-vscid=\"26csir2x4\"><source src=\"https://assets.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt4d8fd8b70257507c/5f3a7a314a5621727d1a365a/video-kibana-enterprise-search-navigation.mp4\"></video>\n</div><p>\t<strong></strong>\n</p><h2><strong>Go from Dashboard to document level data in seconds&#160;with the new explore underlying data feature</strong></h2><p>Dashboards in Kibana provide an easy, yet powerful way to centralize visualizations built on top of Elasticsearch data. Viewers of Kibana dashboards enjoy not just detailed charts, but also the ability to easily interact and filter analyses in order to discover additional insights. In 7.8 we released the Drilldowns capability for Kibana which lets you create custom drill paths between one or more dashboards. In 7.9 we’re going even further by introducing the explore underlying data feature that allows you to simply use a chart’s action panel to go directly to the Discover app so you can investigate the document level details of the chart data you were just looking at — complete with the previously selected filter, query, and time range.&#160;\n</p><p>This new capability works from the context menu of any standard Kibana visualization (including Kibana Lens visuals) based on a single Elasticsearch index. In can also be enabled as an in-chart context menu by editing your kibana.yaml settings file to add: <strong><code>pack.discoverEnhanced.actions.exploreDataInChart.enabled: true</code></strong>\n</p><div class=\"embed-responsive embed-responsive-16by9 shadow m-t-40 m-b-40\"><div class=\"vsc-controller\" data-vscid=\"5mchj5q3l\"></div><video autoplay=\"autoplay\" playsinline=\"playsinline\" muted=\"muted\" loop=\"loop\" data-vscid=\"5mchj5q3l\"><source src=\"https://assets.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/bltcaed233aa861bbd8/5f3ab64131523c5cba9b847e/video-kibana-explore-underlying-data-discover2.mp4\"></video>\n</div><strong><h2>Doing more with Anomaly Explorer visuals in dashboards</h2></strong><p>In 7.8 we made it <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/blog/kibana-7-8-0-released\">possible to add visuals from Anomaly Explorer right into a dashboard</a> just like any other chart to help you build more robust views of your data.&#160;In 7.9 we’ve added three new capabilities to that experience designed to give you and viewers of your dashboards even greater interactivity. The first is the ability to select cells within an embedded Anomaly Explorer visual and use that selection as a time filter for the rest of the dashboard. The second new capability is simply the option to use selected cells as an overall dashboard filter. And lastly, you can now navigate from an embedded Anomaly Explorer view in a dashboard directly to that view in the Anomaly Detection tab within Elastic Machine Learning. All of these new features help you easily integrate visualized machine learning results directly within your dashboards, making it simple to create analyses that offer deeper insights&#160;into&#160;your data.\n</p><div class=\"embed-responsive embed-responsive-16by9 shadow m-t-40 m-b-40\"><div class=\"vsc-controller\" data-vscid=\"1ol53jcc0\"></div><video autoplay=\"autoplay\" playsinline=\"playsinline\" muted=\"muted\" loop=\"loop\" data-vscid=\"1ol53jcc0\"><source src=\"https://assets.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt1cdcceb71c6a02e5/5f3a9b761340d47a564b5d6d/video-ml-anomaly-explorer-swimlane-filters.mp4\"></video>\n</div><strong><h2>Embedding Kibana: New iframe configurations</h2></strong><p>Taking Kibana dashboards and embedding them in your internal wiki or even your external website is a powerful way to put visualized insights from Elasticsearch data in front of hundreds if not thousands or even tens of people. In 7.9, thanks to an outstanding community contribution from <a href=\"https://github.com/ajwild\">Alex Wild</a>, you have greater flexibility to show exactly which elements appear when you embed Kibana via iframe.&#160;Specifically, you can now choose to include or exclude the filter bar, query, time bar, and top menu.\n</p><div class=\"embed-responsive embed-responsive-16by9 shadow m-t-40 m-b-40\"><div class=\"vsc-controller\" data-vscid=\"68wtj704s\"></div><video autoplay=\"autoplay\" playsinline=\"playsinline\" muted=\"muted\" loop=\"loop\" data-vscid=\"68wtj704s\"><source src=\"https://assets.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/bltf86752036ab5960c/5f3a9be70f176f7b9ab29380/video-kibana-embed-configurations.mp4\"></video>\n</div> <strong><h3></h3></strong> <strong><h2>Kibana Lens</h2></strong> <strong><h3>Visually comparing data using multiple y axes</h3></strong><p>Comparing two metrics at the same time is a powerful and quick way to spot outliers. For example, you might be monitoring for potential intrusion threats against your website by tracking something like port scans in one analysis and unique IP addresses in another. Both of these are the right places to be looking, but separately these metrics might not tell the whole story.&#160;The ability to place these two indicators visually on top of each other might be the only way to flag that a single IP is actually conducting a suspiciously high number of the overall port scans against your site for a given time period.\n</p><p>In 7.9 we want to make this kind of data comparison even easier in Kibana Lens, which is why we’re so excited to be rolling out the ability to have two metrics on a y axes at the same time.&#160;Lens has always been able to stack multiple metrics on top of one another (either from the same index or different ones), but with the new multiple y axes capability you no longer have to have those fields share the same left-hand axis. In 7.9 you can control which y axis is measured on the right and which is on the left, giving you the power to compare and contrast data with vastly different ranges.&#160;Using this feature will be especially helpful when looking to compare an aggregated metric (e.g., total pageviews) against a percentage (e.g., webpage bounce rate) since the potentially wide ranged metric whose results could tally in the millions won’t visually drown the percentage whose results are most often between 1 and 100.&#160;\n</p><div class=\"embed-responsive embed-responsive-16by9 shadow m-t-40 m-b-40\"><div class=\"vsc-controller\" data-vscid=\"tmlbpv35c\"></div><video autoplay=\"autoplay\" playsinline=\"playsinline\" muted=\"muted\" loop=\"loop\" data-vscid=\"tmlbpv35c\"><source src=\"https://assets.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt143741567c11478b/5f3a9c4048bdc47f3f1e869d/video-kibana-lens-multiple-y-axes.mp4\"></video>\n</div><strong><h3>Custom color selector</h3></strong><p>Crafting the look of your Kibana Lens visualization just got easier in 7.9 with the new ability to pick a specific color for a field on the y axis. The same format & style tab used to shift an axis to the right or left to achieve multiple y axes also contains a series style input that lets you determine the exact color you’d like a metric to be. You can choose between using the visual color picker or entering in an exact hexadecimal color code if you already know the precise color you’d like to use. If you are segmenting your y axis with another field in the “Breakdown by” area to build a stacked bar chart or draw multiple lines, etc., Lens will still automatically pick which colors are used. However, where you are using multiple independent metrics on the y axis for comparison analytics, using this new custom color selector is highly recommended to help you visually create stark color contrast.\n</p><div class=\"embed-responsive embed-responsive-16by9 shadow m-t-40 m-b-40\"><div class=\"vsc-controller\" data-vscid=\"a1z104uti\"></div><video autoplay=\"autoplay\" playsinline=\"playsinline\" muted=\"muted\" loop=\"loop\" data-vscid=\"a1z104uti\"><source src=\"https://assets.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt63b5dd750348e971/5f3aa18348bdc47f3f1e86ab/video-kibana-lens-y-axes-color-picker.mp4\"></video>\n</div><p><strong></strong>\n</p><h3><strong>Handling data gaps with fitting functions</strong></h3><p>It is not uncommon when analyzing data to come across gaps where there is simply no data available.&#160;Your website was down for maintenance and so there just aren’t any pageviews for a one-hour period or maybe the time range you are examining when investigating suspicious log activity is granular enough that there are large visual gaps between timestamps.&#160;Whatever the situation, it’s important to be able to visually represent these scenarios accurately or you run the risk of accidentally conveying the wrong message.\n</p><p>In 7.9, Lens helps you do this quickly and easily by introducing the chart settings dropdown which includes data fitting functions designed to let you choose exactly how to represent sparse data. Lens’s default is to not fill in data gaps (hide function), however you can now also choose to fill gaps with zeros (zero function), fill gaps with a line (linear function), or fill gaps according to the last or next data point (last and next functions). Going back to the examples above, a website maintenance outage means there were zero pageviews happening and so representing those breaks by showing a dip to zero might be appropriate. In the hunting suspicious log activity example though, being so zoomed in on a time range that the visual is showing the gaps between single minute timestamp updates doesn’t mean there is a dip in data, just that what’s being shown is literally between normal intervals.&#160;In this scenario it might be more appropriate to use the linear or last options to convey that there is in fact no downward trend or missing data. Regardless of the scenario, Kibana Lens in 7.9 is ready to help you paint the right picture when it comes to filling in the gaps (or not).&#160;\n</p><div class=\"embed-responsive embed-responsive-16by9 shadow m-t-40 m-b-40\"><div class=\"vsc-controller\" data-vscid=\"q1im68oih\"></div><video autoplay=\"autoplay\" playsinline=\"playsinline\" muted=\"muted\" loop=\"loop\" data-vscid=\"q1im68oih\"><source src=\"https://assets.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt922d7f4e7346ed8e/5f3aa884db5c28785b6f075c/video-kibana-lens-fitting-function.mp4\"></video>\n</div><strong><h2>Canvas</h2></strong> <strong><h3>Variables: Edit once, update everything</h3></strong><p>It’s no secret that Canvas gives you the power to create stunning visual dashboards and presentations from your data stored in Elasticsearch. In 7.9 we are making the process for building and iterating beautiful Canvas workpads more efficient by introducing variables when writing Canvas expressions. Where previously you might have hardcoded something in the expression editor like the color of a shape or even the name of an index used by a chart or metric, you now have the ability to use the “var” function.&#160;This allows you to reference a string, boolean, or number that acts as an alias. That alias is tied to a specific value (e.g., hexidecimal color code, index name, etc.) and is accessible in the new variables interface outside of the expression editor. Ultimately this makes changing a single variable all that is potentially needed to update multiple Canvas elements simultaneously.&#160;\n</p><p>The new variables feature is incredibly useful especially if you use your Canvas workpads as templates. If you clone or update workpads often and want to efficiently swap style colors, fonts, etc., or have a lightning fast way to simply&#160;point some or all of the elements at a different Elasticsearch index, then you are going to love using variables.\n</p><div class=\"embed-responsive embed-responsive-16by9 shadow m-t-40 m-b-40\"><div class=\"vsc-controller\" data-vscid=\"ya9rud3xw\"></div><video autoplay=\"autoplay\" playsinline=\"playsinline\" muted=\"muted\" loop=\"loop\" data-vscid=\"ya9rud3xw\"><source src=\"https://assets.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt51b83415a5c5e54a/5f3aa8e1327a6201d7ebc36c/video-kibana-canvas-variables-editor.mp4\"></video>\n</div><strong><h2>Elastic Maps</h2></strong> <strong><h3>More out-of-the-box map layers: Network traffic for security practitioners</h3></strong><p>In 7.8 we introduced our first prebuilt solution specific layer in Elastic Maps for observability professionals intended to automatically take APM data and build real user monitoring (RUM) traffic and performance maps in just a few clicks. With 7.9 we're adding a new solution layer for security practitioners, designed to take network data and rapidly build&#160;geography-driven network traffic analyses.&#160;If it feels like you’ve seen something like this before, you have.&#160;This visual is affectionately known as a pew-pew map and already lives as an embedded item in the Network interface for Elastic Security.&#160;Our goal with adding it as a prebuilt layer inside of Elastic Maps is to extend its potential impact for threat hunters with the ability to further customize the analysis using additional data layers as well as make it easier to add to dashboards and Canvas workpads.\n</p><div class=\"embed-responsive embed-responsive-16by9 shadow m-t-40 m-b-40\"><div class=\"vsc-controller\" data-vscid=\"6ugdc4sxf\"></div><video autoplay=\"autoplay\" playsinline=\"playsinline\" muted=\"muted\" loop=\"loop\" data-vscid=\"6ugdc4sxf\"><source src=\"https://assets.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt17ef768454e1a750/5f3aa945ddf9d7332963a61f/video-maps-security-layer.mp4\"></video>\n</div><strong><h3>Building filled maps faster with the new choropleth wizard</h3></strong><p>Heatmap? Filled map? Shaded map? If you’ve ever wondered how exactly to refer to a map where the areas inside the map shapes are colored (e.g., a world map comparing various countries, colored according to a metric like GDP, literacy, military budget, etc.), you are in luck because Elastic Maps 7.9 can tell you. It’s officially called a choropleth map and it’s never been easier to build one in the Elastic Maps app with the new choropleth step-by-step wizard. The new Choropleth option in the “Add layer” menu lets you quickly select the administrative boundaries for your geoanalysis or geo_shapes as defined in one of your Elasticsearch indices.&#160;From there it is just a few more clicks to define what metric governs the coloring of your map shapes and then the standard easy to use Elastic Maps formatting options. A key time saver with this new feature is the way the wizard automatically handles the term joins required to connect the data you want to measure with the data needed to create the map shapes.&#160;\n</p><div class=\"embed-responsive embed-responsive-16by9 shadow m-t-40 m-b-40\"><div class=\"vsc-controller\" data-vscid=\"vp2y7maim\"></div><video autoplay=\"autoplay\" playsinline=\"playsinline\" muted=\"muted\" loop=\"loop\" data-vscid=\"vp2y7maim\"><source src=\"https://assets.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt1737c26d3a3d19c0/5f3aa993ca538c7eaba30455/video-kibana-maps-choropleth-wizard.mp4\"></video>\n</div><strong><h3>Leveraging custom vector data sources</h3></strong><p>While Elastic Maps provides you with an incredible number of options for building and designing maps built on top of your Elasticsearch data, our goal is also to make it simple to bring in your own customizations. That’s why we’re excited in 7.9 to roll out the ability for you to connect to your own vector data sources in order to control both the context and style of your map. With the new Vector Tile option in the “Add Layer” menu, you can quickly add a URL to your vector data, add the name of the layer you would like to use, then apply stylings to match your desired aesthetic.&#160;\n</p><div class=\"embed-responsive embed-responsive-16by9 shadow m-t-40 m-b-40\"><div class=\"vsc-controller\" data-vscid=\"zv4018yk2\"></div><video autoplay=\"autoplay\" playsinline=\"playsinline\" muted=\"muted\" loop=\"loop\" data-vscid=\"zv4018yk2\"><source src=\"https://assets.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt224cd70cc5403126/5f3aa9deddf9d7332963a623/video-maps-vector-tile-service.mp4\"></video>\n</div><h3>Another reason to zoom-in: New global administrative regions added</h3><p>The ability to see provinces, cantons, states, and counties globally just got a lot easier in 7.9 with the release of a new layer for administrative regions. Thanks to this enhancement you can use your global Elasticsearch data to do things like compare website traffic coming from French departments and German states without having to add each country’s administrative levels individually. Or from a security perspective, in just a couple clicks you can have a detailed view of network activity as specific as something like Chilean administrative districts and Argentine provinces. We’ve added nearly 60 countries worth of administrative data in this release with the goal of letting you create the exact geoanalysis you need.\n</p><div class=\"embed-responsive embed-responsive-16by9 shadow m-t-40 m-b-40\"><div class=\"vsc-controller\" data-vscid=\"t4z5hxapu\"></div><video autoplay=\"autoplay\" playsinline=\"playsinline\" muted=\"muted\" loop=\"loop\" data-vscid=\"t4z5hxapu\"><source src=\"https://assets.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt70b23a8f3991f42a/5f3aaa48ddf9d7332963a627/video-maps-administrative-regoins.mp4\"></video>\n</div><strong><h2>Alerting</h2></strong> <strong><h3>Trigger ServiceNow incidents with alerts from the Elastic Stack</h3></strong><p>The release of the new alerting framework in 7.7 introduced an easy way to create alerts in the Elastic Stack and connect to them actionable outcomes like triggering emails, sending messages, or initiating escalations in third-party platforms. With 7.9 we’ve added ServiceNow as a native alerting connector, allowing you to have Elastic Stack alerts create incidents in the ServiceNow platform.\n</p><div class=\"embed-responsive embed-responsive-16by9 shadow m-t-40 m-b-40\"><div class=\"vsc-controller\" data-vscid=\"5nc9py7es\"></div><video autoplay=\"autoplay\" playsinline=\"playsinline\" muted=\"muted\" loop=\"loop\" data-vscid=\"5nc9py7es\"><source src=\"https://assets.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/bltfa1db5a3d31f2cc6/5f3aaaab12fb5b71fd0a1ade/video-alerting-servicenow.mp4\"></video>\n</div><strong><h2>Machine Learning</h2></strong> <strong><h3>Correcting polluted models: Managing snapshots</h3></strong><p>Ever been alerted by Anomaly Detection only to discover after digging in that the culprit is a benign blip in the data causing false alarms? Maybe something went down for maintenance or a project triggered an unusual, but very explainable, set of activity.&#160;Maybe it’s a one-time event so big it is warping baseline results (e.g., Black Friday traffic spike, etc.). Regardless, the result is still a chunk of data feeding into your model and skewing the results.&#160;\n</p><p>In 7.9, fixing this becomes incredibly simple with the new model snapshot management capability accessible directly in the Anomaly Detection user interface that lets you quickly revert back to an earlier snapshot or even just skip the problem events.&#160;Simply navigate to the model snapshots tab in job management and use your mouse to make the updates you need.\n</p><div class=\"embed-responsive embed-responsive-16by9 shadow m-t-40 m-b-40\"><div class=\"vsc-controller\" data-vscid=\"yewuycxas\"></div><video autoplay=\"autoplay\" playsinline=\"playsinline\" muted=\"muted\" loop=\"loop\" data-vscid=\"yewuycxas\"><source src=\"https://assets.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt53a2010e77c6f429/5f3aab0e31523c5cba9b8466/video-ml-model-snapshot-management.mp4\"></video>\n</div><p>While reverting a model to a previous snapshot <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/elasticsearch/reference/current/ml-revert-snapshot.html\">has been possible via our API</a>, bringing this capability directly into the UI and also adding the ability to exclude events in a time range supports our ongoing efforts to make machine learning not just powerful, but also easy enough for everyone to use. With model snapshot management, anyone can quickly get an anomaly detection job back to an effective state. And for that reason, we also want to make it even easier to spot when and how models change (especially when those changes are big) which is why we’re adding some great new features to annotations.&#160;&#160;</p> <strong><h3>Automatically generated Anomaly Detection annotations</h3></strong><p>To help give you more insight into Anomaly Detection jobs, we’re introducing a new feature with 7.9 that automatically creates an annotation when a significant change in the model calculation happens. Examples of things that might trigger this include periodicity or a trend or step change. You’ll still be able to add your own annotations and to help make sure you can tell the difference between a system-generated annotation and a user created one;&#160;we’re also updating the annotation table itself by adding search and filter capabilities.\n</p><div class=\"embed-responsive embed-responsive-16by9 shadow m-t-40 m-b-40\"><div class=\"vsc-controller\" data-vscid=\"dtl1ihjxg\"></div><video autoplay=\"autoplay\" playsinline=\"playsinline\" muted=\"muted\" loop=\"loop\" data-vscid=\"dtl1ihjxg\"><source src=\"https://assets.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt06903ae3b8569085/5f3aab700f176f7b9ab293c8/video-machine-learning-automatic-annotations.mp4\"></video>\n</div><strong><h3>Seeing your data: Preview charts in the Transforms wizard and data frame analytics&#160;</h3></strong><p>Whether you're transforming your data to get it ready for analysis or evaluating your data after running it through a data frame analytics job, understanding the distribution and range of your fields is important. With 7.9 we’ve added the ability to toggle-on histogram charts in the Transform and data frame analytics wizards so that you can quickly understand the shape of your data columns.&#160;All of this is designed to give you richer visibility into your data so you can spot outliers faster as well as more easily compare prediction results vs. actual values.\n</p><div class=\"embed-responsive embed-responsive-16by9 shadow m-t-40 m-b-40\"><div class=\"vsc-controller\" data-vscid=\"f4175gedv\"></div><video autoplay=\"autoplay\" playsinline=\"playsinline\" muted=\"muted\" loop=\"loop\" data-vscid=\"f4175gedv\"><source src=\"https://assets.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt4e441d059fc330c4/5f3aabc4ddf9d7332963a62b/video-machine-learning-data-grid-preview-charts.mp4\"></video>\n</div><strong><h3>More machine learning integrations with Elastic Observability and Elastic Security</h3></strong><p>On top of the great new standalone machine learning capabilities added in 7.9, Elastic solutions continue to weave machine learning capabilities directly into their experiences to better support monitoring and security professionals. Elastic Security now has five new advanced anomaly detection jobs in 7.9 for AWS CloudTrail designed to spot when a malicious actor may have created, updated, or even deleted a log. If you are using the APM application within Elastic Observability you’ll notice in 7.9 that anomaly data is being surfaced in charts as well as in the service maps.\n</p> <strong><h2>Get hands-on with Kibana 7.9</h2></strong><p>Ready to try out some of these new features? Your own personal Kibana 7.9 experience is just minutes away using the free trial option on <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/cloud/\">Elastic Cloud</a>. <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/downloads/elasticsearch\">Downloading the latest builds</a> is also a great way to get started checking out these new capabilities. Find us on Twitter (<a href=\"https://twitter.com/elastic\">@elastic</a>) or in <a href=\"https://discuss.elastic.co/c/kibana\">our forum</a> and let us know what you think. And as always, never hesitate to report any problems on the <a href=\"https://github.com/elastic/kibana/issues\">GitHub issues page</a>.\n</p><p>Related blogs:\n</p><ul>\n\t<li><a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/blog/whats-new-in-elastic-7-9-0-free-workplace-search-engine-endpoint-security\">Elastic 7.9 released</a></li>\n\t<li><a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/blog/whats-new-elasticsearch-7-9-0\">Elasticsearch 7.9 released</a></li>\n\t<li><a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/blog/whats-new-elastic-enterprise-search-7-9-0-free-workplace-search-kibana-access\">Elastic Enterprise Search 7.9 released</a></li>\n\t<li><a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/blog/whats-new-elastic-security-7-9-0-free-endpoint-security\">Elastic Security 7.9 released</a></li>\n\t<li><a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/blog/whats-new-elastic-observability-7-9-0-unified-agent-kpi-overview-dashboard\">Elastic Observability 7.9 released</a></li>\n</ul>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Wed, 19 Aug 2020 16:03:00 +0000","feedId":7403,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"6819dbf56576d40a80412f4e8249e8c9","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"Aris Papadopoulos","publishedOrCreatedDate":1598311822917},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 07:30:22","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 07:30:22","title":"Fed team spotlight: Christopher Brown on training the next line of defense","link":"https://www.elastic.co/blog/culture-fed-team-spotlight-christopher-brown-on-training-the-next-line-of-defense","description":"<p><em>Life @ Elastic celebrates the various ways Elasticians create community, lead by example, and carry out our Source Code in everything they do. In this Spotlight we talk to Christopher Brown about his role as an education architect and what it takes to train the next generation of security professionals. </em>\n</p><h3>Can you tell us about your role?\n</h3><p><strong>Christopher Brown: </strong>I’m an education architect at Elastic. Really, I’m more of a security instructor — I don’t actually do a lot of architecting.\n</p><h3>Where are you located?</h3><p><strong>Christopher: </strong>I’m based out of central Indiana, about 35 or 40 miles out of Indianapolis. I’m out in the middle of a lot of cornfields. I actually live on five acres surrounded by cornfields. We’re just about to get fiber internet.\n</p><h3>Welcome to the 21st century!</h3><p><strong>Christopher: </strong>Traditionally I’m a road warrior and the hotel Wi-Fi has always been faster than at home. Now that I’m home all the time, it’s a relief to get something a bit faster.\n</p><h3>What does training mean in your job?</h3><p><strong>Christopher: </strong>It typically entails delivery of training and teaching students. In our case it's usually to the Department of Defense, government, and federal employees.\n</p><h3>How did you get into the training space? Specifically, how did you get into the federal security work that you do?</h3><p><strong>Christopher: </strong>I come from a family of consultants and trainers. My mom was a cosmetology instructor. My dad worked with Raytheon for 30 years. He was a defense contracting and field engineer instructor. My brother worked for Apple education for 10 years. We were all somewhere between consulting and training. I was a Microsoft trainer until 2004. Around that time I started having students in my class from government and federal roles. I remember a few of them at the time saying, “You know, you would be interested in our work.” One person in particular slid me a business card and said we should speak offline. He happened to be with a company called TAMSCO which did technical account management and security consulting. They were bought by a group, DRS, and in 2006 I went on my first overseas assignment with them.\n</p><h3>What initially got you interested in the security field?</h3><p><strong>Christopher: </strong>When I was at Microsoft I was given a book called <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Cuckoo%27s_Egg?baymax=web&elektra=culture-fed-team-spotlight-christopher-brown-on-training-the-next-line-of-defense\"><i>The Cuckoo’s Egg</i> by Clifford Stoll</a> and I was hooked. Stoll was doing a systems administration job on a time-sharing system that happened to have some minor accounting inaccuracies. He wanted to get to the bottom of it. So he began investigating and it turned out to be international hackers. The book details how he unraveled the whole case. I found it interesting. I think a lot of people get started this way — they’re not in security but they find the mystery interesting and just want to keep digging deeper and deeper.\n</p><h3>What drew you to Elastic?</h3><p><strong>Christopher: </strong>There were a few things. I kind of knew the team that I was going into because we've been running in very similar circles. My current team has a lot of people from <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/blog/welcome-perched-security-training-for-siem-threat-hunting-and-more?baymax=web&elektra=culture-fed-team-spotlight-christopher-brown-on-training-the-next-line-of-defense\">Perched, which joined forces with Elastic last year</a>. They are a lot of former Army, Air Force, and Navy professionals, people who develop training — so I’d encountered them in previous roles. <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/blog/endgame-joins-forces-with-elastic?baymax=web&elektra=culture-fed-team-spotlight-christopher-brown-on-training-the-next-line-of-defensew\">Endgame</a> came in later and now both teams have merged and are responsible for delivering federal services education. It’s been a really good fit for me.\n</p><h3>What excites you about the role right now?</h3><p><strong>Christopher: </strong>Digging into students’ minds and figuring out how they really learn, because every student is different. To move out of the theoretical and academic world of teaching principles to really help someone to understand the topic.\n</p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt2f43f998c622edb0/5f32b0269751e50576cbbdcb/image1.jpg\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt2f43f998c622edb0\" alt=\"Christopher Brown in the classroom\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto;\">\n</p><p>A lot of the courses that we have for network security are capture the flag related. So we don't ask questions that involve a straight answer, or looking one up. Instead, students really have to search for the right answer. It pushes students to get out of their comfort zone. It gets them out of routine work where they sit in a lab and write down all the info and that’s it. I don’t think that’s an effective way to learn. Finding these teaching solutions really excites me.\n</p><h3>How many students do you usually have in the classroom?</h3><p><strong>Christopher: </strong>It’s usually a bigger class load, between 20 and 30. Really an ideal class is around 12 students but that’s not often possible.\n</p><h3>What’s been one of the biggest challenges of modifying your work during COVID-19?</h3><p><strong>Christopher: </strong>Not travelling, because I’m so used to it being part of the job. I’m 48 and I’m working on passport number three. I’ve never been grounded like this before, and I hope to travel again someday.\n</p><h3>How have you adapted your classroom for a virtual setting?</h3><p><strong>Christopher: </strong>We’d run a few classes in the past virtually using Strigo and Slack to interact with students live. But there’s one big adaptation. You need to really listen when working virtually. There are a lot of instructors that feel they need to talk all the time. This is fine in a live setting where you can read whether a student is losing interest and adapt. But in a virtual setting it isn’t always possible to read your students. I mean, you can ask them questions and get some feedback in chat and that kind of thing, but you don’t normally see them since many keep their video off.\n</p><p>When I see you on video, and you smile and nod, I can see that you’re paying attention. I’m getting that feedback. But without video on that’s impossible. So asking questions and listening is very important.\n</p><h3>Do you have any other tips or tricks for teaching virtually?</h3><p><strong>Christopher: </strong>It’s important not to stretch out lectures on theory as long as you would in a live classroom. Keep it to 10- to 15-minute chunks and get them into the lab. You have to keep students active to hold their interest.\n</p><h3>What does success look like for your team?</h3><p><strong>Christopher: </strong>I think success for our team is really just putting together a program or a structure where students can come in and learn what it takes to keep their organization secure. And, you know, maybe six months down the road we can check in with them and see how they're doing. If they're happy, and they can say, hey, you know, we learned something and caught a big threat — that’s real success.\n</p><p><em>Interested in joining Elastic? We’re hiring. Check out <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/about/teams/?baymax=web&elektra=culture-fed-team-spotlight-christopher-brown-on-training-the-next-line-of-defense\">our teams</a> and <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/about/careers/?baymax=web&elektra=culture-fed-team-spotlight-christopher-brown-on-training-the-next-line-of-defense\">find your fit!</a> Want to read more about life at Elastic? Read more <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/blog/category/culture?baymax=web&elektra=culture-fed-team-spotlight-christopher-brown-on-training-the-next-line-of-defense\">on our blog!</a></em>\n</p>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Wed, 12 Aug 2020 15:00:00 +0000","feedId":7403,"bgimg":"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt2f43f998c622edb0/5f32b0269751e50576cbbdcb/image1.jpg","linkMd5":"b126a7f9143e6e390e62a08f17dd94e0","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx8/cdn49@2020_6/2020/08/24/23-30-23-288_d23c0828f44c0d81.webp","destWidth":1999,"destHeight":1331,"sourceBytes":213891,"destBytes":170668,"author":"Elastic Culture","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt2f43f998c622edb0/5f32b0269751e50576cbbdcb/image1.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx8/cdn49@2020_6/2020/08/24/23-30-23-288_d23c0828f44c0d81.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598311822917},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 07:30:22","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 07:30:22","title":"Introduction to Windows tokens for security practitioners","link":"https://www.elastic.co/blog/introduction-to-windows-tokens-for-security-practitioners","description":"<p>This blog series is aimed at giving defense practitioners a thorough understanding of Windows access tokens for the purposes of detection engineering.&#160;</p><p>Here in Part 1, we'll cover key concepts in Windows Security.&#160;The desired outcome is to help defenders understand how access tokens work in Windows environments.&#160;In Part 2 of the&#160;series, we’ll build on the concepts outlined in Part 1&#160;and cover&#160;how attackers abuse legitimate Windows <a href=\"https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1134/\">functionality</a> to move laterally and compromise entire Active Directory domains in depth.</p><p>As a note of caution, this blog has deliberately attempted to abstract away the workings of specific Windows network authentication protocols (e.g., NTLM and Kerberos) and Security Support Providers (e.g., CredSSP, Negotiate, etc.). As a consequence, there may be instances where behavior unique to these protocols/packages differs with the behavior described below (i.e., Kerberos constrained delegation). Additionally, this blog owes a huge debt of gratitude to <i>Programming Windows Security</i> by Keith Brown which is a fantastic resource for learning more about fundamental Windows security concepts.\n</p> <strong><h2>Logon sessions and access tokens</h2></strong><p>The key concept to grasp in order to understand authentication in Windows environments is the relationship between <strong>logon sessions</strong> and <strong>access tokens</strong>. A logon session is used to represent the <em>presence</em> of a user on a machine and begins when a user is successfully authenticated and ends when the user logs off.\n</p><p>For example, when a user physically logs on to a Windows workstation (i.e., interactively), they supply a username and password, which is then checked by the <a href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/windows-server/security/windows-authentication/credentials-processes-in-windows-authentication\">Local Security Authority</a> (LSA). If the account is a local account (i.e., only valid on that specific computer) the LSA will check the credentials against its own security database. In the case of a Windows Active Directory domain environment, the authentication attempt is referred to the closest domain controller (DC) which will process the request and authenticate the user.\n</p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/bltc5d931ae03cda6fb/5f3c5f2b6bbc05271064339a/1-windows-logon-session-access-blog-windows-tokens-for-defenders.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"bltc5d931ae03cda6fb\" alt=\"1-windows-logon-session-access-blog-windows-tokens-for-defenders.png\">\n</p><figcaption>Figure 1 - Windows logon process for an interactive user</figcaption><p>Once the user has been successfully authenticated, the LSA will create a new <strong>logon session</strong> and produce an <strong>access token</strong>, as shown above.<sup>1</sup>&#160;A logon session can have multiple access tokens associated with it, but an access token can only ever be linked to one logon session (which is typically the successful logon attempt that generated it).<sup>2</sup>&#160;Windows has legitimate functionality which can be used to change the logon session (and hence cached credentials) that your current token is associated with. This will be covered in more detail in part 2.</p><p><span></span>Every new logon session is identifiable via a 64 bit locally unique identifier (LUID), referred to as the logon ID, and every access token must contain an Authentication Id (or AuthId) parameter that identifies the origin/linked logon session via this LUID. This is highlighted in the diagram below:</p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt5e0d9d711f5c3946/5f3c5f38752d292b6ca4e530/2-authid-parameter-blog-windows-tokens-for-defenders.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt5e0d9d711f5c3946\" alt=\"2-authid-parameter-blog-windows-tokens-for-defenders.png\">\n</p><figcaption>Figure 2 - Every access token is linked to a sole logon session, which is identifiable via the AuthID parameter. The AuthID field contains a 64 bit LUID, or logon ID, which identifies the <strong>origin</strong> logon session.\n</figcaption><p><br>\n</p><p>The main function of an access token is to act as a “volatile repository for security settings associated with the logon session” which can be adjusted and modified on the fly.<sup>2</sup>&#160;In this sense, access tokens act as a <strong>proxy</strong> or <strong>stand-in</strong> for the logon session and so when making security decisions, Windows developers never interact with the logon session itself (which is “hidden” away in lsass), but with an access token which <strong>represents</strong> it (and hence predominantly via the Windows access token API).&#160;\n</p><p>Therefore, a developer can copy existing tokens (<a href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-gb/windows/win32/api/securitybaseapi/nf-securitybaseapi-duplicatetokenex?redirectedfrom=MSDN\">DuplicateTokenEx</a>), modify the security settings for a given token (<a href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/api/securitybaseapi/nf-securitybaseapi-gettokeninformation\">Get</a>/<a href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/api/securitybaseapi/nf-securitybaseapi-settokeninformation\">SetTokenInformation</a>) etc.&#160;to their heart's content, but these tokens are still just abstractions representing the security settings from the<em> originating</em> logon session.\n</p><p>Most importantly, the access token represents the <strong>security context</strong> of the user. The security context can be defined as the privileges and permissions that a user has on a specific workstation (and across the network). An access token caches a number of <a href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/secauthz/access-tokens\">attributes</a> which determine its security context, such as:\n</p><ul>\n\t<li>The security identifier (SID) for the user</li>\n\t<li>Group memberships</li>\n\t<li>Privileges held</li>\n\t<li>A logon ID which <em>references</em> the<strong> origin</strong> logon session</li>\n</ul><p>For example, the screenshot below shows the cached security attributes for an access token using James Forshaw’s <a href=\"https://github.com/googleprojectzero/sandbox-attacksurface-analysis-tools\">TokenViewer</a>:\n</p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/bltcbe78d08b45c817d/5f3c5f43327a6201d7ebc9cf/3-cached-security-attributes-blog-windows-tokens-for-defenders.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"bltcbe78d08b45c817d\" alt=\"3-cached-security-attributes-blog-windows-tokens-for-defenders.png\">\n</p><figcaption>Figure 3 -&#160;TokenViewer revealing the cached security attributes stored in an access token</figcaption><p><span id=\"docs-internal-guid-77ec5f66-7fff-f8af-45f9-e0532a87e770\">As discussed previously, the Authentication ID parameter, which is the key link between an access token and the logon session that it represents, contains a 64 bit LUID (logon ID) which identifies the origin logon session that this access token is associated with. Note also&#160;that it is possible to infer a number of other conclusions about the state of this token, e.g., it is a primary token, it is not elevated (medium integrity),&#160;<span id=\"docs-internal-guid-8c74de17-7fff-f1c9-dba1-c7f851ccaaee\">and the user is an administrator (<a href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/api/winnt/ne-winnt-token_elevation_type\">Elevation Type</a> = limited means the token is a ‘filtered’ admin token and hence UAC is enabled)</span>.</span><br>\n</p><p>Whenever a thread <a href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/secauthz/interaction-between-threads-and-securable-objects\">attempts to access a securable object</a> managed by the Windows kernel, such as a process, thread, handle, semaphore, token, etc., Windows will perform an <a href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/api/securitybaseapi/nf-securitybaseapi-accesscheck\">access check</a>. To perform this check, Windows needs three pieces of information<sup>2</sup>:\n</p><ul>\n\t<li><strong>Who</strong> is requesting access?</li>\n\t<li><strong>What</strong> are their intentions with the object?</li>\n\t<li><strong>Who</strong> can access the object?</li>\n</ul><p>Hence, Windows will first check the token associated with the calling thread and look at the authorization attributes cached in it (e.g., user sid, group memberships, privileges etc.). Secondly, Windows will look at the desired <a href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-gb/windows/win32/secauthz/access-rights-and-access-masks\">access</a> requested by the thread. In the Windows security model you <strong>must</strong> state your intentions upfront; for performance reasons an access check only occurs <em>once</em> and no further checks are performed on any additional handle operations (unless a user attempts to perform an action that the handle did not have rights to, e.g., write to a read-only handle).&#160;Thirdly, Windows will retrieve the <a href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/secauthz/security-descriptors\">security descriptor</a> for the target object. The security descriptor contains a discretionary access control list (<a href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/secauthz/access-control-lists\">DACL</a>) which specifies what users/groups have access to the object and the <em>type </em>of access granted.&#160;\n</p><p>Based on these three sources of information, Windows can give a boolean answer to whether a principal has access to a given object. This is why every process <strong>must</strong> have a primary token; it is the user that is “charged” for any objects that process attempts to access.<sup>2</sup>\n</p><p>As a note, some <a href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/secauthz/privilege-constants\">privileges</a> can be thought of as simply enabling a user to bypass/skip the access check in the kernel for a given object. For example, if a token has the <a href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/api/processthreadsapi/nf-processthreadsapi-openprocess\">SeDebugPrivilege</a> privilege enabled, the Windows kernel will skip the DACL checks for any process and thread objects (hence why it is so powerful).<sup>3</sup>\n</p><p>Following successful authentication from an interactive logon, Windows will execute the user’s shell (normally explorer.exe) on behalf of the newly logged-on user. The operating system performs this action by using the newly minted access token to spawn explorer.exe as that user via <a href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/api/processthreadsapi/nf-processthreadsapi-createprocessasusera\">CreateProcessAsUserA</a>. This function takes a handle to a token and spawns a new process as the user specified in the token (i.e., in a <em>different</em> security context).\n</p><p>Typically, every process created by the user is a child of the shell process (i.e., explorer.exe)<sup>4</sup>&#160;and every new process will (by default) run in the <strong>same</strong> security context as its parent; hence the child process will inherit its parent’s access token upon creation.<sup>5</sup>&#160;Therefore, <em>all</em> processes will inherit their <em>own</em> local copy of an access token.\n</p><p>As stated previously, access tokens act as a local “volatile repository” for the security settings associated with the logon session. As each process has its <em>own</em> local copy of an access token, a process can modify the volatile security settings stored in its copy without affecting other processes.<sup>2</sup>\n</p><p>For example, a browser such as Chrome may want to create a <em>restricted</em> version of its access token in order to effectively <a href=\"https://chromium.googlesource.com/chromium/src/+/master/docs/design/sandbox.md\">sandbox</a> the application in the event an attacker is able to exploit the browser and obtain arbitrary code execution on the compromised machine. As a consequence of the sandbox, any actions performed by the attacker will be restricted and help prevent further damage. As previously discussed, <em>all</em> access checks in Windows make decisions based on the attributes stored in the calling thread’s token, and so by ‘hardening’ the token a developer can restrict its access.\n</p><p>The key point is that Chrome can modify its local copy of the token <em>without</em> affecting other applications. This can be achieved via APIs such as <a href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/api/securitybaseapi/nf-securitybaseapi-adjusttokengroups\">AdjustTokenGroups</a>/<a href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/api/securitybaseapi/nf-securitybaseapi-adjusttokenprivileges\">AdjustTokenPrivileges</a>, which can be used to <a href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/secauthz/enabling-and-disabling-privileges-in-c--\">disable</a> dangerous groups and privileges, respectively. Alternatively, a new restricted copy of a specified access token can be created with <a href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/api/securitybaseapi/nf-securitybaseapi-createrestrictedtoken\">CreateRestrictedToken</a>.&#160;As an example, the relevant functionality in the chromium source code can be <a href=\"https://github.com/chromium/chromium/blob/4e88a3c4fa53bf4d3622d07fd13f3812d835e40f/sandbox/win/src/restricted_token.cc#L65\">found here</a>.\n</p><p>This is so important because, as discussed above, access tokens are the <strong>core</strong> component of the Windows security model and so by being able to change the information cached in them, a developer can limit <strong>what</strong> securable objects a token can touch and hence restrict its access across a system.\n</p><p>The diagram below summarizes the logon process and an example&#160;access check for an interactive user, <strong>ASTRO\\cosmo</strong>:\n</p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt7b021068d99d5ea2/5f3c5f8760261e2e581f214f/4-logon-process-access-control-blog-windows-tokens-for-defenders.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt7b021068d99d5ea2\" alt=\"4-logon-process-access-control-blog-windows-tokens-for-defenders.png\">\n</p><figcaption>Figure 4 -&#160;An overview of the Windows logon process and access checks for an interactive logon</figcaption><p> <strong></strong>\n</p><h2><strong>Network authentication</strong></h2><p>Having covered local authentication and access control, what happens under the hood when a user needs to access some resource located across the network? For example, a user could attempt to view the available shares on another host by running the following command:\n</p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blteb93047fd50346e0/5f3c5f93327a6201d7ebc9d3/5-attempt-available-shares-blog-windows-tokens-for-defenders.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blteb93047fd50346e0\" alt=\"5-attempt-available-shares-blog-windows-tokens-for-defenders.png\">\n</p><figcaption>Figure 5 -&#160;Attempt to view available shares on the remote domain controller, \\\\MISTAKENOT-DC-1</figcaption><p>The user’s logon session is <strong>unique</strong> to their workstation (as is their access token and privileges) and they cannot simply send their access token over the wire. The token would be meaningless as it <em>does not</em> correspond to a valid logon session on the remote host. Furthermore, this authentication mechanism would be an obvious target for replay attacks.<sup>2</sup>\n</p><p> In this case, the user needs to <strong>re-authenticate</strong> and establish a <strong>new</strong> logon session on the remote machine (assuming the user has access). For an interactive logon (and actually all other logon types like service, batch, etc.,&#160;<em>except</em> network<sup>6</sup>) Windows will <strong><em>automatically</em></strong> cache the credentials as part of the Windows single sign-on (SSO) mechanism.<sup>7</sup>&#160;This is the intended design of the Windows SSO mechanism and prevents the user from having to constantly re-enter their password when accessing network resources.\n</p><p>As a consequence, access tokens which link back to these types of logon sessions <em>can</em> authenticate to remote hosts and Windows will <strong>automatically</strong> authenticate on the users behalf whenever a network resource is accessed by a thread or process.<sup>8</sup>&#160;Note that Windows will <strong>always</strong> use the credentials cached in the logon session that the access token is linked to when authenticating remotely&#160;(e.g., Windows will find the token’s linked logon session, via the AuthId, and use the credentials cached for that logon session, as shown below).\n</p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt985c90d304c8a2e9/5f3d9001004c131277a52a66/access-token-linked-session-blog-windows-tokens-for-defenders.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt985c90d304c8a2e9\" alt=\"access-token-linked-session-blog-windows-tokens-for-defenders.png\" \"=\"\">\n</p><p><span id=\"docs-internal-guid-da53e0ed-7fff-736c-9c82-4809789dbc4b\"><span style=\"font-size: 11pt; font-family: Arial; color: rgb(0, 0, 0); background-color: transparent; font-variant-numeric: normal; font-variant-east-asian: normal; vertical-align: baseline; white-space: pre-wrap;\">\n\t</span></span>\n</p><figcaption>Figure 6 - Relationship between an access token, its linked logon session, and the credentials cached for that logon session</figcaption><p>Therefore, in order to establish a new logon session, the SMB server will need to authenticate the client over the network. In Windows domains, network authentication is typically performed via <a href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/windows-server/security/kerberos/kerberos-authentication-overview\">Kerberos</a> or the legacy challenge-response protocol <a href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/secauthn/microsoft-ntlm\">NTLM</a>. Irrespective of the network authentication protocol used, on receiving an authentication request the target host will forward the credential information to the <a href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/secauthn/key-distribution-center\">DC</a> and, following successful authentication, establish a new <em>network</em> login session for the user (i.e.,&#160;<strong>this login</strong> “<strong>represents a remote client”</strong>).<sup>2</sup>&#160;&#160;\n</p><p>Network logins <strong>do not</strong> cache credentials and therefore you cannot use this token to authenticate to another remote host.<sup>9</sup>&#160;This is commonly referred to as the ‘double hop’ problem. Note that due to the inherent <a href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/secauthn/microsoft-ntlm\">design</a> of the NTLM challenge response protocol (e.g., the client encrypts a challenge with the user’s NTLM hash) it fundamentally does not support credential delegation.\n</p><p>Most importantly from the server’s perspective, following the successful authentication of the remote user, it is presented with a newly minted<em> </em><strong>access token</strong> which represents the network logon of the remote client. The diagram below illustrates this process:\n</p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/bltc34cb1078b03baab/5f3c5fa0752d292b6ca4e534/6-network-logon-blog-windows-tokens-for-defenders.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"bltc34cb1078b03baab\" alt=\"6-network-logon-blog-windows-tokens-for-defenders.png\">\n</p><figcaption>Figure 7 - The network authentication process when a user attempts to access a remote resource, such as an SMB file share\n</figcaption><p>This neatly leads to the second key concept for Windows access tokens: <a href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/com/impersonation\"><strong>impersonation</strong></a>. As previously mentioned, access tokens encode a wealth of information about the security context of the user and enable a handy way for developers to make “localized” changes to this context <em>without</em> affecting other processes.<sup>2</sup>&#160;However, in <strong>multi-threaded</strong> applications, problems and difficult-to-debug race conditions may arise if different threads start enabling/disabling different privileges or modifying default token DACLs.<sup>2 10</sup></p><p>As a result, Windows has a feature called impersonation. By default <em>all</em> threads will inherit the same security context as their process’s primary token. However, impersonation allows a thread to switch to a <em>different</em> security context.&#160;Specifically, it enables threads to have their own local copy of a token; known as an impersonation token.&#160;This is the best way to remember the distinction between primary and impersonation tokens, in that impersonation tokens are always applied to threads, whereas primary tokens are associated with processes. In this way, the SMB server can handle each incoming client request in a separate thread and impersonate the access token representing the remote client.</p><p>Also note that switching security context has two implications. The first is that&#160;<strong>locally</strong> the thread is now impersonating a different access token, and hence any <strong>local</strong> access checks will be performed using this new token. Secondly, as this impersonated token may be linked to a <strong>different </strong>logon session (and therefore potentially have <em>different</em> cached credentials (if a non-network login)) the thread’s security context <strong>remotely</strong> is also <em>different</em>. Furthermore, as we shall see in the next blog post in this series, do not always assume that the cached credentials stored in lsass <strong>match</strong> the user specified in the token.\n</p><p>In summary, from the perspective of a listening server process (say an SMB file server), the following steps must occur following a connection request from a remote client:\n</p><ul>\n\t<li>The user is authenticated and a new logon session is created (<strong>NETWORK_ONLY</strong>)</li>\n\t<li>The server process is presented with a handle to an impersonation token which links back to the remote client’s new<strong> network logon session</strong></li>\n\t<li>The server can use this token to impersonate the client to perform work on their behalf</li>\n</ul><p>This approach has the added benefit of making use of the existing Windows’ access control model, as all actions performed while impersonating are under the security context of the user’s identity (hence any local access check decisions will use the information cached in the impersonated user’s token). Therefore, if that user does not already have access to a specific file on a share, they will be denied access.\n</p><p>For most of Windows’ key IPC mechanisms (e.g., named pipes, RPC, COM) this process is handled automatically. The server needs only to call the appropriate API in order to obtain a handle to the remote clients’ security context (i.e., access token) and start impersonating the client via functions such as<sup>2</sup>:\n</p><ul>\n\t<li dir=\"ltr\"><a href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/api/securitybaseapi/nf-securitybaseapi-impersonateloggedonuser\">ImpersonateLoggedOnUser</a></li>\n\t<li dir=\"ltr\"><a href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/api/combaseapi/nf-combaseapi-coimpersonateclient\">CoImpersonateClient</a></li>\n\t<li dir=\"ltr\"><a href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/api/rpcdce/nf-rpcdce-rpcimpersonateclient\">RpcImpersonateClient</a></li>\n\t<li dir=\"ltr\"><a href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/api/namedpipeapi/nf-namedpipeapi-impersonatenamedpipeclient\">ImpersonateNamedPipeClient</a></li>\n\t<li dir=\"ltr\"><a href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-gb/windows/win32/api/ddeml/nf-ddeml-ddeimpersonateclient\">DdeImpersonateClient</a></li>\n</ul> <strong><h2>Conclusion</h2></strong><p>Hopefully this has been an informative overview of some key concepts in Windows Security. Stay tuned for Part 2 of this blog series, where we’ll build on the concepts outlined above and cover in more depth how attackers abuse legitimate Windows <a href=\"https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1134/\">functionality</a> to move laterally and compromise entire Active Directory domains, and how you can detect and respond to access token manipulation within your environment.\n</p><p>Ready for holistic data protection with <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/security\">Elastic Security</a>? Try it free today, or experience our latest version on <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/elasticsearch/service\">Elasticsearch Service</a> on Elastic Cloud.\n</p><p><br>\n</p><h2>References</h2><ol>\n\t<li><span id=\"docs-internal-guid-6316c506-7fff-03e8-4fa6-11c2f82df0e5\">With <a href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/security/identity-protection/user-account-control/how-user-account-control-works\">UAC</a> enabled, Windows actually creates two tokens for an administrative user: a filtered user token and a ‘linked’ administrative token. dashboard","link":"https://www.elastic.co/blog/whats-new-elastic-observability-7-9-0-unified-agent-kpi-overview-dashboard","description":"<p><a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/blog/whats-new-in-elastic-7-9-0-free-workplace-search-engine-endpoint-security\"></a>We are pleased to announce version 7.9 of the Elastic Observability solution, adding&nbsp;several exciting new capabilities that focus on reducing time to value for observability teams. Our latest version adds a single, unified agent for collecting all kinds of telemetry data that dramatically simplifies data onboarding down to just a few clicks; a new observability overview page that surfaces key insights across all your telemetry data and provides a curated at-a-glance view of the health of your technology stack; and 50+ turnkey security detections that help security teams get instant value from observability data.&nbsp; </p>\n<p>Experience the latest version of Elastic Observability on our <a href=\"http://cloud.elastic.co/\">Elasticsearch Service on Elastic Cloud</a> (a free <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/elasticsearch/service\">14-day trial</a> is available) or <a href=\"https://elastic.co/downloads\">install the latest version</a> of the Elastic Stack for a self-managed experience.&nbsp; </p>\n<p>And now, without further ado, here are the release highlights.&nbsp; </p>\n<h2>Onboarding data gets radically simpler with a single, unified agent</h2>\n<p>Data ingest is a critical — and often time-consuming — piece of observability and security workflows. How easy is it to instrument the system you want to monitor? Is the collected data parsed and structured to be usable? How quickly can you visualize and get key insights from this data? Now multiply this by the thousands of components — hosts, containers, applications, middleware, and more —&nbsp; in your environment, and the operational aspects such as deployment, configuration, and upgrades become just as critical. </p>\n<p>Our vision is to simplify the ingest process down to one agent, one-click so that observability engineers can spend more time acting on insights, and less time managing their ingest to get to the insight. We are excited to announce that we are taking our first step (a really BIG step) towards achieving this vision with the launch of a single unified agent, along with several new ingest capabilities in version 7.9.&nbsp; </p>\n<p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt6e8978d7677327f3/5f355f23c9ec57071816f580/ingest-manager.gif\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt6e8978d7677327f3\" alt=\"ingest-manager.gif\"> </img></p>\n<p>These new capabilities are in beta in 7.9, and we invite our users to try these features out and <a href=\"https://discuss.elastic.co/c/observability/82\">provide us with feedback</a>.&nbsp;<br> </br></p>\n<ul> \n <li>Elastic Agent, one agent to collect it all:&nbsp;Elastic Agent is a single, unified way to collect all kinds of telemetry data from a host. Elastic Agent (beta) supports logs, metrics, and endpoint security data collection in 7.9, with plans to expand to include APM and other data types in the future. Having a single agent to install, configure, update, upgrade, and maintain is a huge efficiency boost for operators. And, with a single configuration file, adding or removing sources is just a click away.&nbsp;</li> \n</ul>\n<ul> \n <li>Integrations for popular services and platforms: 7.9 has support for ~40 integrations, with plans to port all 100+ Beats modules over in the next few releases. Integrations ship with prepackaged content like dashboards and a data transformation pipeline that let you go from data to insight in literally minutes.</li> \n</ul>\n<ul> \n <li>Centrally manage all your agents with Fleet — the control tower for all deployed agents:&nbsp;A typical enterprise will usually have agents deployed on tens of thousands of hosts. Our goal with Fleet is to make it easy for operators to manage this spread from a single place. When you make a change to a configuration, all the agents using that configuration will get the new configuration, often within seconds, simplifying the operational aspects even further. Goodbye SSH and automation playbooks.&nbsp;</li> \n</ul>\n<ul> \n <li>Ingest Manager, one UI to control i(nges)t all:&nbsp;Ingest Manager (beta) is a brand new Kibana app to control all aspects of your ingest universe from a central place. Create, edit, and update your agent configuration using the convenient Configuration Editor. So long, YAML files.</li> \n</ul>\n<ul> \n <li>Ingest Node Pipelines UI, a more visual and intuitive way to parse logs: The benefits of schema on write — parsing and extracting fields from unstructured logs before indexing them — <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/blog/schema-on-write-vs-schema-on-read\">is well established</a>. Even as we have added more out-of-the-box integrations that automatically extract structured fields for common sources, we recognize that users will always need the flexibility to parse and transform custom log formats. In 7.9, we are making this process much simpler and approachable through the general availability of the Ingest Node Pipeline UI in Kibana. Adios, JSON files.&nbsp;</li> \n</ul>\n<p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/bltbcb4112aba75cda5/5f355f82ddf9d73329639c25/ingest-pipeline.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"bltbcb4112aba75cda5\" alt=\"ingest-pipeline.png\"><br> </br></img></p>\n<p>Read more about these features in this <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/blog/introducing-elastic-agent-and-ingest-manager\">detailed blog</a>. Note that given the beta status of these features (with the exception of Ingest Node Pipeline UI), we recommend against using these in production environments.<br> </br></p>\n<h2>Enhancing analyst experience with a unified observability overview page</h2>\n<p>Unification of the three pillars of observability (logs, metrics, and traces) at the data layer is a core differentiator of Elastic Observability. A single datastore for all your observability data is essential for cross-source investigative workflows and helps lower mean time to resolution.&nbsp; </p>\n<p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt789a1b5bb67c4a28/5f355f9c48d3b57cb974a5d8/observability-overview.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt789a1b5bb67c4a28\" alt=\"observability-overview.png\"> </img></p>\n<p>Building on this unified data foundation, we are excited to extend that unification to the visualization layer with the launch of a new observability overview page in Kibana. The landing page presents a curated at-a-glance view of the health of your entire ecosystem by bubbling up key insights from all data streams (logs, metrics, traces, etc.) across all sources (apps, infrastructure, etc.).&nbsp; While you could always create this type of view using the flexible dashboarding features in Kibana, this out-of-the-box page gets you there faster — especially for new users or new setups. The overview page also embeds helpful widgets like newsfeed and&nbsp;training to enhance your in-product experience.&nbsp;<br> </br></p>\n<h2>Embracing open standards with the OpenTelemetry integration in Elastic APM&nbsp;</h2>\n<p>From open code to open community, Elastic is built on transparency. That mindset extends to our support for open standards in the observability space such as OpenTracing, Jaeger, and W3C Trace-Context. We are happy to add the recently formed OpenTelemetry standard to that list. Our goal is to enable users that have instrumented their system using these methods to migrate their apps to Elastic with minimal effort.&nbsp; </p>\n<p>OpenTelemetry is a Cloud Native Computing Foundation (CNCF) sandbox project (currently in beta) that provides vendor-neutral, language-specific agents, SDKs, and APIs to collect all observability data (such distributed traces, metrics, and logs) from all your monitored elements. We have added the Elastic Exporter (and contributed it to the <a href=\"https://opentelemetry.io/registry/collector-exporter-elastic/\">OpenTelemetry collector contrib repo</a>) that takes the trace data collected using OpenTelemetry Collector, translates it to Elastic compatible protocol, and sends it to Elastic APM. This means that you can start exploring your OpenTelemetry data in Elastic APM with zero changes to your instrumentation. Just configure the Elastic Exporter into your OpenTelemetry setup and start exploring your trace data in Elastic APM, alongside other logs, metrics, and traces, in minutes.&nbsp; </p>\n<p>OpenTelemetry is a fast evolving project, and will expand to include metrics and logs. As a member of the OpenTelemetry project, we are following the roadmap closely and will enhance our support to match their progress. Read more about our <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/blog/elastic-apm-opentelemetry-integration\">OpenTelemetry integration here.</a> </p>\n<h2>Surfacing security signals in observability data with 50+ prebuilt detections</h2>\n<p>As organizations migrate to the cloud or adopt new tools, security remains a top concern. Each new technology expands the threat envelope, and adds one more element that security teams need to monitor. The good news is that much of the logs and metrics data your operations teams are collecting for health and performance monitoring also includes rich information that your security teams can use to detect malicious behaviors. In 7.9, we are making it possible for security teams to mine those threat signals from observability data in minutes, with zero effort, by launching 50+ turnkey security detections.&nbsp; </p>\n<p>The turnkey detections introduced in this release focus on five major detection areas — asset visibility, data protection, identity and access monitoring, log audit, and network monitoring — across two popular platforms, AWS and Okta. For example, this includes a group of unsupervised machine learning jobs to detect uncommon AWS API calls based on user, location, or errors generated. Such a rule could have detected and prevented a recent attack on large financial institutions that used AWS API calls to compromise data.&nbsp; </p>\n<p>Another example is identity and access monitoring detections on Okta logs to identify attackers who are trying to tamper with MFA configurations, API tokens, or other critical account parameters. And of course, with the flexible detection engine you are welcome to create additional rules to fit your environment. </p>\n<p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt775c5eff6f952826/5f3563c6168ec908bebea5d3/detection-rules-alt.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt775c5eff6f952826\" alt=\"detection-rules-alt.png\"> </img></p>\n<p>Read more about these security integrations <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/blog/whats-new-elastic-security-7-9-0-free-endpoint-security\">in this post</a>. </p>\n<h2>Additional highlights</h2>\n<p>Anomaly detection alerting and monitor availability in Uptime:&nbsp;In Elastic Uptime 7.9, users will now be able to see the availability percentage of their monitors (both overall and per-testing node), enabling them to quickly report service-level objectives. Users will also be able to configure alerting to get notified if their availability falls below a specific value. Elastic Uptime 7.9 adds machine learning features that automatically detect if a monitor is experiencing anomalous response durations from one or more testing nodes.&nbsp; </p>\n<p>Azure Monitoring enhancement: Our cloud observability story has rapidly advanced in the last few releases with the launch of several new integrations into the Azure, Google Cloud, and AWS ecosystems. In this release, we are continuing to expand and simplify the Azure monitoring use case with the launch of two new integrations: <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/beats/metricbeat/7.9/metricbeat-metricset-azure-billing.html\">Azure Billing</a> and <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/beats/metricbeat/7.9/metricbeat-metricset-azure-app_insights.html\">Azure Application Insights</a>.&nbsp; </p>\n<h2>Try it out today</h2>\n<p>To start taking advantage of these new features and capabilities, upgrade your Elasticsearch Service deployment to 7.9, or grab a <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/elasticsearch/service\">free 14-day trial</a> of our Elasticsearch Service on Elastic Cloud or install the <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/start\">latest version</a> of the Elastic Stack. </p>\n<h2>Related blogs</h2>\n<ul> \n <li><a
