+0000","feedId":7403,"bgimg":"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt6e8978d7677327f3/5f355f23c9ec57071816f580/ingest-manager.gif","linkMd5":"71fe4533ddcde0b31e47fbdc7ec2018c","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx18/cdn42@2020_4/2020/08/24/23-30-43-606_0f4e697f567d1ff9.webp","destWidth":1920,"destHeight":1080,"sourceBytes":2740817,"destBytes":2312306,"author":"Asawari Samant","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt6e8978d7677327f3/5f355f23c9ec57071816f580/ingest-manager.gif":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx18/cdn42@2020_4/2020/08/24/23-30-43-606_0f4e697f567d1ff9.webp","https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/bltbcb4112aba75cda5/5f355f82ddf9d73329639c25/ingest-pipeline.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn20@2020_3/2020/08/24/23-30-47-134_dfc0a46c027b0797.webp","https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt789a1b5bb67c4a28/5f355f9c48d3b57cb974a5d8/observability-overview.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn67@2020_5/2020/08/24/23-30-46-188_35276b56a28b2905.webp","https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt775c5eff6f952826/5f3563c6168ec908bebea5d3/detection-rules-alt.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx24/cdn63@2020_2/2020/08/24/23-30-47-921_d1a61a2066c0064b.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598311822916},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 07:30:22","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 07:30:22","title":"Security operations: Cloud monitoring and detection with Elastic Security","link":"https://www.elastic.co/blog/cloud-monitoring-and-detection-with-elastic-security","description":"<p><strong></strong>\n</p><p>As many organizations have migrated their infrastructure, applications, and data to cloud offerings, adversaries have extended their operational capabilities in cloud environments to achieve their mission — whether that means stealing intellectual property, disrupting business operations, or holding an organization’s data for ransom. In order to protect our users’ data from attack, the Elastic Security&#160;Intelligence & Analytics Team researches and develops <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/blog/elastic-security-opens-public-detection-rules-repo\">rules</a> to detect attacker behavior in the cloud <em>and</em> on the endpoint.\n</p><p>In this post, we’ll discuss cloud monitoring and detection-related challenges security operations teams face, and why attacks against cloud environments are often successful. We will share details on our free cloud detection rules (including many new ones released in <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/blog/whats-new-elastic-security-7-9-0-free-endpoint-security\">Elastic Security 7.9</a>) and show how they can help <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/security\">Elastic Security</a> users.\n</p><p>We’ll also explain how Elastic can ingest logs from a wide variety of cloud platforms and how the Elastic Common Schema (ECS) makes searching, monitoring, and detection easy for defenders.\n</p> <strong><h2><strong>Cloud monitoring and detection challenges</strong></h2></strong><p>Security teams typically encounter one or more of the following challenges when they’re asked to monitor, detect, and respond to threats in their organization's cloud environments:\n</p><ul>\n\t<li><strong>Resource constraints:</strong>&#160;It can take a considerable amount of time to learn and understand cloud technologies and their ever-changing data sources. Many security operations teams do not have the resources to allocate to this ongoing effort.</li>\n\t<li><strong>Understanding of adversary tradecraft:</strong>&#160;Attacker behavior on well-known platforms such as Windows has been researched extensively and shared with the security community. Security teams may not have an in-depth understanding of how adversaries operate in cloud environments or the ability to provision a test environment to practice offensive and defensive techniques to protect their organization.</li>\n\t<li><strong>Blind spots:</strong>&#160;For effective monitoring and detection, the data available to security practitioners must be relevant, accurate, and timely. Cloud logs shipped to a SIEM can be used for detection and response as long as the security team can depend on the quality of the data.</li>\n\t<li><strong>Data normalization:</strong>&#160;Most cloud platforms have their own log categories and event schema. Normalizing logs into a common schema is not a trivial or one-off task. Some security teams, for example, have several different field names for a hostname across their data sources indexed in their SIEM. Without a normalized and documented schema, it can be difficult for analysts — especially less experienced ones — to write search queries and correlate events across data sources effectively.</li>\n</ul> <strong><h2><strong>Ingesting and searching cloud logs with Elastic</strong></h2></strong><p>Elastic has a large collection of Filebeat <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/beats/filebeat/current/filebeat-modules.html\">modules</a> that can be used to simplify the collection, parsing, and visualization of many diverse log formats into a common schema — including cloud platforms such as <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/beats/filebeat/current/filebeat-module-aws.html\">Amazon Web Services (AWS)</a>, <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/beats/filebeat/current/filebeat-module-azure.html\">Azure</a>, <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/beats/filebeat/current/filebeat-module-okta.html\">Okta</a>, and <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/beats/filebeat/current/filebeat-module-o365.html\">Office 365</a>. Rapid development of new Filebeat modules is an ongoing process.\n</p><p>The&#160;<a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/ecs/current/ecs-reference.html\">Elastic Common Schema</a> (ECS) defines a common set of fields for ingesting logs from a connected data source (e.g., AWS/Okta) into Elasticsearch. Log data is normalized into a format where the various field names can be used in queries to correlate behavior across data sources. This is useful to security and IT operations teams for a number of reasons.\n</p><p>Practitioners and administrators do not need to spend countless hours transforming or normalizing their ingested logs so that the field names follow their own common schema. Managing a schema like this yourself is no small undertaking and is a continuous effort. Elastic manages ECS (saving users time and resources) so that security teams can rely on a common set of field names to search their data quickly and efficiently.\n</p><p>End users can rely on using the same field names in their queries when searching across multiple data sources, which presents the following advantages:\n</p><ul>\n\t<li>Having a consistent schema for searching saves security analysts time and lowers the barrier to entry for new analysts. Analysts don’t have to learn or remember all of the different field names and their purpose for each data source.</li>\n\t<li>Analysts can correlate events across data sources such as endpoint, proxy, and firewall, which helps them ask questions of their data more efficiently and make sound decisions during an investigation, incident, or hunt.</li>\n\t<li>It’s easy for analysts to produce a timeline or build a visualization of the activity that occurred.</li>\n</ul> <strong><h2><strong>Detecting attackers operating in cloud environments</strong></h2></strong><p>The Elastic Security Intelligence & Analytics Team’s research into adversary tradecraft leads to new detection features like rules and machine learning jobs — capabilities that enable small security teams to have an outsized impact. Security features like these increase the cost of an attack for adversaries. Elastic Security users can expect to see a continued focus on increasing the cost of cloud attacks.\n</p><p>In the remainder of this blog post, we’ll simulate attack techniques against AWS and Okta cloud environments. We’ll review the alerts that are generated by the suspicious activity and how an analyst can perform initial triage and complete their investigation using Elastic Security. We will also demonstrate how analysts can add exceptions to detection rules in order to filter benign events and continue to alert on suspicious behavior.\n</p> <strong><h2><strong>Monitoring AWS CloudTrail logs to detect suspicious behavior</strong></h2></strong><p>As organizations migrate to or provision new infrastructure in cloud platforms like AWS, they face the common challenges that we described earlier. Fortunately, Elastic Security has a <a href=\"https://github.com/elastic/detection-rules/tree/main/rules/aws\">strong variety of AWS rules</a>, available for <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/blog/whats-new-elastic-security-7-9-0-free-endpoint-security\">free in 7.9</a> to detect suspicious behaviors in an AWS environment.\n</p><p>The Filebeat <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/beats/filebeat/master/filebeat-module-aws.html\">module</a> for AWS helps you easily ship CloudTrail, Simple Storage Service (S3), Elastic Load Balancing (ELB), and virtual private cloud (VPC) flow logs to Elasticsearch for monitoring and detection in Elastic Security. Let’s walk through an attack and defense scenario utilizing CloudTrail data. <a href=\"https://aws.amazon.com/cloudtrail/\">CloudTrail</a> provides event history of your AWS account activity, including actions taken through the AWS Management Console, AWS software development kits (SDKs), command line tools, and other AWS services. This event history can help simplify security detection, analysis, and investigations.\n</p><p>Many attacks against AWS start with an attacker obtaining an access key and/or the secret access key details. These keys may be harvested in a variety of ways, including through phishing, a data breach, GitHub repositories, screenshots, error messages, snapshot data, or simply poor key management practices. By obtaining these keys, an attacker can take a variety of actions against your AWS infrastructure.\n</p><p>Let’s walk through one of the many potential attack scenarios that could play out. In the following example, the adversary enumerates the trails and monitoring capabilities that have been configured for the AWS account. They follow up on this activity by disabling a trail and a configuration recorder in an attempt to evade detections and then proceed to harvest secrets.\n</p> <strong><h3><strong><em>Simulating adversary behavior in AWS</em></strong></h3></strong><p>In this demonstration, we’ll use <a href=\"https://github.com/RhinoSecurityLabs/pacu\">Pacu</a> to perform our attack. Pacu is a popular framework for exploiting AWS infrastructure, developed and maintained by Rhino Security Labs. Pacu is modular, similar to other exploitation frameworks like Metasploit and Koadic, and enables attackers to exploit configuration flaws within an AWS account. Attackers can use Pacu to check if the required permissions are assigned to the compromised account before attempting to execute a module. This can be helpful from an attacker's perspective to not create unnecessary noise and logs, and draw additional attention from defenders by running modules that will ultimately fail.\n</p><p>The attacker begins by enumerating services using the <code>detection__enum_services</code> module to determine what logging and monitoring services are enabled for the AWS account.\n</p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt5cf78b83c1322fab/5f3c385c4f4b6324ce744fcd/1-enumerating-services-blog-secops-cloud-platform-monitoring.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt5cf78b83c1322fab\" alt=\"1-enumerating-services-blog-secops-cloud-platform-monitoring.png\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto; border:1px solid black\">\n</p><figcaption>Figure 1 - Enumerating services using Pacu’s detection__enum_services module\n</figcaption><p>The attacker discovered eight trails, as well as ten configuration rules, a recorder, and a delivery channel. Essentially, the enumeration script is querying certain AWS API calls to list or describe relevant information about the environment. By reviewing the <a href=\"https://github.com/RhinoSecurityLabs/pacu/blob/master/modules/detection__enum_services/main.py\">code</a> of the module, we can see the targeted APIs:\n</p><pre>DescribeSubscription\nGetSubscriptionState\nDescribeTrails\nListDetectors\nDescribeConfigRules\nDescribeConfigurationRecorders\nDescribeConfigurationRecorderStatus\nDescribeDeliveryChannels\nDescribeDeliveryChannelStatus\nDescribeConfigurationAggregators\nDescribeAlarms\nDescribeFlowLogs\n</pre><p>After the attacker determines which services are running, their next logical step may be to interrupt logging and monitoring by disabling a trail, alarm, detector, or recorder in an attempt to evade detection. To accomplish this objective, we’ll use a different module called <code>detection__disruption</code> to disable a trail called <code>brentlog</code>, and stop the configuration recorder named <code>default</code>.\n</p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt4722e6ec69a693e7/5f3c386c6e9660261c55b7df/2-disabling-trail-blog-secops-cloud-platform-monitoring.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt4722e6ec69a693e7\" alt=\"2-disabling-trail-blog-secops-cloud-platform-monitoring.png\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto\">\n</p><figcaption>Figure 2 - Disabling a trail and stopping a configuration recorder using Pacu’s detection__disruption module\n</figcaption><p>At this point, with trail logging suspended and the configuration recorder turned off from tracking changes to resources, the attacker may want to check if there are any credentials, API keys, or tokens available in <a href=\"https://aws.amazon.com/about-aws/whats-new/2018/04/introducing-aws-secrets-manager/#:~:text=AWS%20Secrets%20Manager%20is%20a,other%20secrets%20throughout%20their%20lifecycle.\">Secrets Manager</a> and if so, collect them. In this scenario, the attacker uses the <code>enum_secrets</code> module and finds one secret in the directory, <code>/sessions/brent/downloads/secrets/secrets_manager</code>. Harvesting these secrets could help the adversary achieve lateral movement and/or privilege escalation.\n</p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt1d8ccf8245d26532/5f3c387a6e9660261c55b7e3/3-searching-aws-blog-secops-cloud-platform-monitoring.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt1d8ccf8245d26532\" alt=\"3-searching-aws-blog-secops-cloud-platform-monitoring.png\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto; border:1px solid black\">\n</p><figcaption>Figure 3 - Searching for AWS secrets using Pacu’s enum__secrets module\n</figcaption><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt702afec7fc2ff8b5/5f3c38825f7d2953ae8216da/4-viewing-aws-blog-secops-cloud-platform-monitoring.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt702afec7fc2ff8b5\" alt=\"4-viewing-aws-blog-secops-cloud-platform-monitoring.png\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto; border:1px solid black\">\n</p><figcaption>Figure 4 - Viewing the AWS secret after its discovery\n</figcaption><p>We’ll stop our fictitious attack scenario here, but if you’re curious to learn what the attacker could do next, the following Google search will return some examples: <code>intitle:\"AWS\" intext:(\"attack\" | \"breach\")</code>. In the next section, we’ll look at what this behavior looks like from a defender's perspective and how Elastic Security can be used to detect this behavior.\n</p> <strong><h3><strong><em>Detecting and investigating the suspicious behavior in AWS</em></strong></h3></strong><p>While monitoring the usage of the previously mentioned APIs, it can be difficult to distinguish benign activity from suspicious behavior, such as an attacker enumerating an environment. In production environments, monitoring for calls to these APIs can be noisy, as the behavior is quite common. To help find this rare and potentially suspicious behavior, and in addition to the AWS detection rules we have available, we’ve released <a href=\"https://github.com/elastic/detection-rules/tree/main/rules/ml\">machine learning</a> jobs in 7.9 specifically for AWS CloudTrail that help identify outliers, such as patterns of unusual activity that are hard to find using conventional detection rules.\n</p><p>Looking at our detections page from the previous attack, we can see multiple alerts were triggered. Our free built-in detection rules identified the techniques of <em>suspending a trail</em>, <em>stopping a configuration recorder</em>, and <em>grabbing sensitive information from the secrets manager</em>. The other alerts are from the machine learning jobs of <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/security/7.9/unusual-city-for-an-aws-command.html\"><em>Unusual Country For an AWS Command</em></a> and <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/security/master/unusual-aws-command-for-a-user.html\"><em>Unusual AWS Command for a User</em></a> which identify a geolocation (country) that is unusual for the command or a user context that does not normally use the command.\n</p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt050763578d22353b/5f3c388e03cfa41d970edac1/5-viewing-detection-alerts-blog-secops-cloud-platform-monitoring.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt050763578d22353b\" alt=\"5-viewing-detection-alerts-blog-secops-cloud-platform-monitoring.png\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto; border:1px solid black\">\n</p><figcaption>Figure 5 - Viewing the detection alerts in Elastic Security\n</figcaption><p>If we pivot into one of the machine learning alerts, we can see a description of what it detected, along with a built-in investigation guide to walk an analyst through a potential workflow when analyzing an unusual CloudTrail event.\n</p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt6bfb4fdfaafbe98d/5f3c389e4aa1361f48ee1d0b/6-machine-learning-alert-blog-secops-cloud-platform-monitoring.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt6bfb4fdfaafbe98d\" alt=\"6-machine-learning-alert-blog-secops-cloud-platform-monitoring.png\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto; border:1px solid black\">\n</p><figcaption>Figure 6 - Viewing the details of a machine learning alert\n</figcaption><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt743cc5ea2e315dc0/5f3c38b44f4b6324ce744fd1/7-viewing-investigation-notes-blog-secops-cloud-platform-monitoring.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt743cc5ea2e315dc0\" alt=\"7-viewing-investigation-notes-blog-secops-cloud-platform-monitoring.png\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto; border:1px solid black\">\n</p><figcaption>Figure 7 - Viewing the investigation notes for an unusual CloudTrail event\n</figcaption><p>Let's also take a look at the details in the Timeline view from the <em></em><a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/security/master/aws-configuration-recorder-stopped.html\"><em>AWS Configuration Recorder Stopped</em></a> alert. The fields I’m particularly interested in are the API call, user agent string, user identity type, request parameters, and the raw text of the entire event.&#160;\n</p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt6575d758bcd7a869/5f3c38c41020421e0b077b16/8-alert-details-timeline-blog-secops-cloud-platform-monitoring.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt6575d758bcd7a869\" alt=\"8-alert-details-timeline-blog-secops-cloud-platform-monitoring.png\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto; border:1px solid black\">\n</p><figcaption>Figure 8 - Analyzing the alert details in the Timeline\n</figcaption><p>By analyzing the alert, we’re able to quickly determine:<span></span>\n</p><table style=\"width:100%\">\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n\t<td style=\"background-color:#F6F7FA\">\n\t\t<p><strong>Field</strong>\n\t\t</p>\n\t</td>\n\t<td style=\"background-color:#F6F7FA\">\n\t\t<p><strong>Description</strong>\n\t\t</p>\n\t</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n\t<td>\n\t\t<p><strong>event.action</strong>\n\t\t</p>\n\t</td>\n\t<td>\n\t\t<p>Tells us the AWS API call that was made, <code>StopConfigurationRecorder</code>\n\t\t</p>\n\t</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n\t<td style=\"background-color:#F6F7FA\">\n\t\t<p><strong>request_parameters</strong>\n\t\t</p>\n\t</td>\n\t<td style=\"background-color:#F6F7FA\">\n\t\t<p>Gives us the details about what was sent in the request, in our case, the configuration recorder name, <code>default</code>\n\t\t</p>\n\t</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n\t<td>\n\t\t<p><strong>user.name</strong>\n\t\t</p>\n\t</td>\n\t<td>\n\t\t<p>Informs us as to who made the request, <code>pacu</code>\n\t\t</p>\n\t</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n\t<td style=\"background-color:#F6F7FA\">\n\t\t<p><strong>user_identity.type</strong>\n\t\t</p>\n\t</td>\n\t<td style=\"background-color:#F6F7FA\">\n\t\t<p>Contains details about the type of Identity and Access Management (IAM) identity. In our case, an <code>IAMUser</code>. Root is another user identity type we have built in rules for.\n\t\t</p>\n\t</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n\t<td>\n\t\t<p><strong>user_agent</strong>\n\t\t</p>\n\t</td>\n\t<td>\n\t\t<p>The value of the HTTP User-Agent header. User agent strings can be easily modified, but if an account typically uses the AWS Java SDK for their API calls, and it changes, then the detection of the anomalous user agent string can be a quick win.\n\t\t</p>\n\t</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n\t<td style=\"background-color:#F6F7FA\">\n\t\t<p><strong>event.original</strong>\n\t\t</p>\n\t</td>\n\t<td style=\"background-color:#F6F7FA\">\n\t\t<p>Gives us the raw alert details\n\t\t</p>\n\t</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table><figcaption>Table 1 - Analysis of alert fields\n</figcaption><p>After analyzing the alert, we can start to piece together the events and look at what actions the user took just before our alerts fired (and afterwards as applicable). Again, we can spot the attackers enumeration here as well.&#160;\n</p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt933670b641c2318d/5f3c38d6a2c59c1c931a723c/9-event-history-blog-secops-cloud-platform-monitoring.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt933670b641c2318d\" alt=\"9-event-history-blog-secops-cloud-platform-monitoring.png\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto; border:1px solid black\">\n</p><figcaption>Figure 9 - Viewing event history for the user <em>Pacu</em> in the Timeline\n</figcaption><p>We may also want to search our environment for specific API calls to see if they were invoked by other users or hosts, from different IPs, or at other time frames that would be suspicious in our environment.\n</p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt2cec62a87b3387c7/5f3c38e182d2265b5e30edbc/10-api-history-blog-secops-cloud-platform-monitoring.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt2cec62a87b3387c7\" alt=\"10-api-history-blog-secops-cloud-platform-monitoring.png\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto; border:1px solid black\">\n</p><figcaption>Figure 10 - Viewing API call history for the <em>StopConfigurationRecorder</em> API in the Timeline\n</figcaption><p>We can also create a visualization to look for the least common API calls in our environment and pivot from there. For AWS, the API calls are in the <code>event.action</code> field.&#160;\n</p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt7faef8146489f8d4/5f3c38eb82d2265b5e30edc0/11-visualization-api-calls-blog-secops-cloud-platform-monitoring.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt7faef8146489f8d4\" alt=\"11-visualization-api-calls-blog-secops-cloud-platform-monitoring.png\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto; border:1px solid black\">\n</p><figcaption>Figure 11 - Using a visualization to look for least common API calls in our environment\n</figcaption><p>As demonstrated, our free built-in rules for AWS can detect this activity as well as a number of other potential attack scenarios. We’ve opened up our <a href=\"https://github.com/elastic/detection-rules\">rules repository</a> and encourage you to have a look and learn how to <a href=\"https://github.com/elastic/detection-rules#how-to-contribute\">contribute</a> if interested.&#160;\n</p> <strong><h2><strong>Detecting suspicious behavior in Okta logs</strong></h2></strong><p><a href=\"https://www.okta.com/products/single-sign-on/\">Okta single sign-on (SSO)</a> is a cloud solution that allows users to log into a variety of systems in their organization via a centralized process using a single user account. Informing end users that they only have to remember one username and password instead of ten or more reduces the risk that they’ll adopt poor password hygiene and enables system administrators to enforce stronger password policies. Further, multi-factor authentication (MFA) policies can be configured in Okta, which raises the barriers to entry for attackers. Many attackers will simply move on to look for an easier target when they discover that MFA is enforced for their target’s network or user account.\n</p><p>While SSO solutions can provide a convenient user experience and reduce cybersecurity risk for an organization, these centralized systems that offer a type of skeleton key to many systems and applications are often an attractive target for attackers. For example, if an adversary manages to harvest an Okta administrator’s credentials or API token, they could attempt to perform any of the actions in the non-exhaustive list below:\n</p><ul>\n\t<li>Modify or disable MFA policies for one or more applications in order to weaken their victim’s security controls.</li>\n\t<li>Create new user accounts or API tokens to maintain persistence in their target’s environment and attempt to “blend in” and evade detection.</li>\n\t<li>Modify, delete, or deactivate an Okta network zone to loosen the restrictions on which geolocation users or administrators can login from.</li>\n\t<li>Delete or disable an application or other configuration to create a Denial-of-Service (DoS) condition and impact a company’s business operations.</li>\n</ul><p>To enable security teams to monitor their Okta environment for suspicious activity, our <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/beats/filebeat/current/filebeat-module-okta.html\">Okta Filebeat module</a> can pull <a href=\"https://developer.okta.com/docs/reference/api/system-log/\">Okta System Log</a> events and ship them to Elasticsearch to be indexed. Okta’s System Log records events related to an organization in order to provide an audit trail that can be used to understand platform activity. The Elastic Security Intelligence & Analytics Team has <a href=\"https://github.com/elastic/detection-rules/tree/main/rules/okta\">free rules</a> to detect suspicious activity in Okta logs and will continue adding more in future.\n</p><p>In the following example, imagine that an adversary has harvested an API token after gaining initial access to an organization’s network. The API token has administrator privileges and the adversary executes some actions in their target’s Oka environment:\n</p><ul>\n\t<li>Create a new user account and assign administrative permissions to it in order to maintain a presence in the target environment should the security team discover that the current API token is compromised</li>\n\t<li>Deactivate a sign-on policy in order to weaken the target’s security controls</li>\n\t<li>Disable a network zone to enable attackers to authenticate from any geographical location during their intrusion</li>\n</ul><p>The Okta Filebeat module was configured to ship Okta System Log events to Elasticsearch and our Okta rules were activated in Elastic Security. The suspicious activity triggered three alerts shown in Figure 12 below.\n</p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt073d5134f84714ac/5f3c38f882d2265b5e30edc4/12-okta-alerts-blog-secops-cloud-platform-monitoring.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt073d5134f84714ac\" alt=\"12-okta-alerts-blog-secops-cloud-platform-monitoring.png\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto; border:1px solid black\">\n</p><figcaption>Figure 12 - Okta alerts in Elastic Security generated by suspicious activity\n</figcaption><p>Clicking on one of the alerts allows the analyst to review more information about the rule, including the description of the behavior that the rule detects, severity and risk scores, and the associated MITRE ATT&CK® tactic and technique. The analyst can scroll further down the page and begin to investigate the alert in Timeline.\n</p><p>To learn more how Elastic supports ATT&CK, see our presentation: <a href=\"https://youtu.be/2Hh5spqA6bw\">How to Plan and Execute a Hunt</a>.\n</p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/bltf0f11485cff2a2d3/5f3c39016e9660261c55b7e7/13-rule-information-blog-secops-cloud-platform-monitoring.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"bltf0f11485cff2a2d3\" alt=\"13-rule-information-blog-secops-cloud-platform-monitoring.png\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto; border:1px solid black\">\n</p><figcaption>Figure 13 - Viewing a rule’s information and settings\n</figcaption><p>Security practitioners know that every organization’s network is different. Behavior that looks suspicious in one environment may be benign in another. To help security teams find the proverbial “signal in the noise,” users can add exceptions to their detection rules to filter benign events and continue to alert on suspicious events. Figure 14 shows an exception being added to an Okta rule.\n</p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt226d6fc7cd588a0d/5f3c390c4f4b6324ce744fd9/14-adding-exception-blog-secops-cloud-platform-monitoring.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt226d6fc7cd588a0d\" alt=\"14-adding-exception-blog-secops-cloud-platform-monitoring.png\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto; border:1px solid black\">\n</p><figcaption>Figure 14 - Adding an exception to a rule in Elastic Security\n</figcaption><p>We’ve also introduced the “threshold” rule type. Threshold rules aggregate query results and generate an alert when the number of matched events exceeds a certain threshold. The example rule below will generate an alert when 25 Okta user authentication failures occur from a single source IP address. This can be indicative of a brute force or password spraying attack.\n</p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt15af9b4ab23da286/5f3c391652dd0a2af5ff9596/15-okta-brute-force-blog-secops-cloud-platform-monitoring.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt15af9b4ab23da286\" alt=\"15-okta-brute-force-blog-secops-cloud-platform-monitoring.png\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto; border:1px solid black\">\n</p><figcaption>Figure 15 - Reviewing a threshold rule configured to detect an Okta brute force attack\n</figcaption><p>Viewing an alert generated by a threshold rule in the Timeline allows an analyst to review the events that triggered the rule and begin their triage process or investigation.\n</p><p style=\"text-align: center;\"><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt5c59cfde9a49afa0/5f3c392082d2265b5e30edc8/16-reviewing-alert-blog-secops-cloud-platform-monitoring.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt5c59cfde9a49afa0\" alt=\"16-reviewing-alert-blog-secops-cloud-platform-monitoring.png\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto; border:1px solid black\">\n</p><figcaption>Figure 16 - Reviewing an alert from a failed Okta authentication threshold rule in Timeline\n</figcaption><p> <strong></strong>\n</p><h2><strong><strong>Conclusion</strong></strong></h2><p>According to Verizon’s latest <a href=\"https://enterprise.verizon.com/resources/reports/dbir/\">Data Breach Investigations Report</a>, cloud assets were involved in 24% of the report’s 3,950 data breaches reviewed last year. As organizations continue to migrate their data and business operations to the cloud, we can expect this number to increase.\n</p><p>In this blog post, we discussed some of the challenges that security teams face when attempting to monitor for, detect, and investigate suspicious behavior in their organization’s cloud environments. We walked through some practical examples on how attackers operate in cloud environments and how Elastic Security can detect those techniques.\n</p><p>The Elastic Security Intelligence & Analytics Team researches adversary tradecraft and develops new detection rules and machine learning jobs for multiple platforms including cloud. Our users can expect to see our continued focus on increasing the cost of cloud attacks.\n</p><p>Configuring our <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/beats/filebeat/current/filebeat-modules.html\">Filebeat modules</a> to ship logs to Elasticsearch and enable detection rules in Elastic Security is easy. Our <a href=\"https://github.com/elastic/detection-rules\">free detection rules</a> help security teams monitor those logs and detect suspicious behavior, regardless of the size of their team. Elastic Security enables analysts to triage and investigate those alerts quickly and efficiently.\n</p>If you’re interested in learning more about Elastic Security, you can <a 07:30:22","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 07:30:22","title":"What’s new in Elastic Cloud for 7.9: AWS PrivateLink and FedRAMP","link":"https://www.elastic.co/blog/whats-new-elastic-cloud-aws-privatelink-fedramp","description":"<p>There’s a lot that’s new across our managed and self-managed cloud technology in the Elastic Stack 7.9 timeframe, which is nicely summarized in the <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/blog/whats-new-in-elastic-7-9-0-free-workplace-search-engine-endpoint-security\">7.9 release blog</a>. Onward to the cloud!\n</p><h2>Elastic Cloud security and compliance</h2><h3>Network Security: AWS PrivateLink support and IP filtering&#160;</h3><p>Network security is a critical component of Elastic Cloud’s security posture. We launched support for AWS PrivateLink, which securely connects a customer Virtual Private Cloud (VPC) to private AWS services endpoints, and to your Elastic Cloud deployments. We also launched support for IP filtering across all supported public cloud providers, enabling you to specify network access to your Elastic Cloud deployment based on IP addresses, address blocks, and ranges. This ensures restricted access to trusted connections, networks, organizations, and more.&#160;IP filters are deployable in combination with AWS PrivateLink endpoints for additional security on your Amazon Web Services deployment(s). To learn more, watch this seven-minute demonstration to learn about how PrivateLink integration works.\n</p><!-- The script tag should live in the head of your page if at all possible --><!-- Put this wherever you would like your player to appear --><p><img class=\"vidyard-player-embed\" src=\"https://play.vidyard.com/78P8dTvrtmDfLxvffaNaVK.jpg\" data-uuid=\"78P8dTvrtmDfLxvffaNaVK\" data-v=\"4\" data-type=\"inline\" alt=\"AWS PrivateLink integration demo\" style=\"width: 100%; margin: auto; display: block;\">\n</p><figcaption>AWS PrivateLink integration demo</figcaption><h3>FedRAMP authorization</h3><p>Elastic Cloud is generally available in the AWS GovCloud (US-East) environment, and is <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/about/press/elastic-achieves-fedramp-authorization\">authorized</a> for FedRAMP Moderate impact level.&#160;FedRAMP provides standardized security requirements for cloud products, services assessments, and monitoring used in federal agencies. It standardizes security requirements across federal agencies, allowing those agencies to choose authorized cloud solutions. Today, many government agencies are required by law to buy only FedRAMP-authorized solutions for handling information in the cloud. Non-federal organizations also appreciate the certification, because it makes it easier to decide where and how a solution can meet their security needs.\n</p><p>Elastic Cloud implemented more than 325 required security controls specified by the FedRAMP certification process. Some of the controls and technologies used by Elastic Cloud include encryption algorithms, upgraded VPN, Content Delivery Network (CDN), SSH, Proxy, Vulnerability Management and Identity solutions, as well as a robust logging environment. These improvements are now part of Elastic Cloud, included to help you protect your data.&#160;\n</p><p>Of course, achieving <a href=\"https://fedramp.gov/understanding-baselines-and-impact-levels/\">Moderate</a> Impact level means Elastic can also accommodate data needing a Low Impact level. Moderate is the baseline from which we will pursue higher security certifications and FedRAMP compliance on other cloud providers like Azure and Google Cloud. Federal, state, and local governments — as well as higher education institutions and organizations storing government data — can sign up for a <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/federal/cloud\">free 30-day trial</a> today.&#160;\n</p> <strong><h3>Sign up with your Google Account</h3></strong><p>We also added support for Google Accounts, so that you can sign up for Elastic Cloud using your existing Google Account credentials. By using your Google identity to access your Elastic Cloud account there is no need to maintain another set of credentials. It's just one less thing for you to manage.\n</p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/bltbf30048d22047193/5f3604e6c9ec57071816f7eb/Elastic-Cloud-Log-in-with-Google.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"bltbf30048d22047193\" alt=\"Sign up with your Google Account\" \"=\"\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto;\">\n</p><figcaption>Sign up with your Google Account</figcaption><h2><strong>Streamlined purchasing&#160;</strong></h2> <strong><h3>New self-service monthly subscriptions</h3></strong><p>You can now purchase Gold and Platinum monthly subscriptions directly within the Elastic Cloud console. With just a few clicks, you’ll get access to the exclusive capabilities of the Elastic Stack, including our solutions for enterprise search, observability, and security as well as business-critical, SLA-backed technical support.&#160;\n</p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/bltb6d134bfebc8ac60/5f361bd3578b8833eed82f46/subscription_change_elastic_cloud_console.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"bltb6d134bfebc8ac60\" alt=\"Choosing an Elastic Cloud subscription\" \"=\"\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto;\">\n</p><figcaption>Choosing an Elastic Cloud subscription</figcaption><p>Monthly billing allows you to analyze and adjust your subscription usage without entering into a long-term commitment. This flexibility can be useful as you determine which subscription makes&#160;the most sense for your use case and how much of the service you need to consume. When your deployment size and feature requirements become clear, you can switch and commit to an annual subscription with deeper discounts.&#160;\n</p><p><strong></strong>\n</p><h2><strong>Improving service performance</strong></h2><p>In-place configuration changes allow for faster and more reliable configuration updates. Their speed and reliability come from applying changes to the cluster (like settings, upgrades, and resizing) in place, which is followed by a rolling restart of its nodes. This avoids potentially long-running data migration operations inherent with the <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/cloud-enterprise/current/ece-change-deployment.html\">grow-and-shrink</a> approach.&#160;\n</p><p>While there are still some changes that will always require the grow-and-shrink approach, we’re improving Elastic Cloud to use in-place configuration changes wherever possible. When you change your deployment’s configuration, Elastic Cloud will choose the right way to apply the changes, using either the grow-and-shrink approach or in-place configuration changes as appropriate.\n</p><!-- The script tag should live in the head of your page if at all possible --><!-- Put this wherever you would like your player to appear --><p><img class=\"vidyard-player-embed\" src=\"https://play.vidyard.com/PWmPzULwfWi9Wd4HXwipjv.jpg\" data-uuid=\"PWmPzULwfWi9Wd4HXwipjv\" data-v=\"4\" data-type=\"inline\" data-autoplay=\"1\" data-loop=\"1\" data-hidden_controls=\"1\" alt=\"In-place configuration updates\" style=\"width: 100%; margin: auto; display: block;\">\n</p><figcaption>In-place configuration updates</figcaption><p>Plus, Amazon EC2 M5d general purpose and R5d memory-optimized instances are now used in all supported AWS regions to improve performance.<br>\n</p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/bltbe833c29ff5eeb00/5f36a86a7ca22b018aed979c/elastic_cloud_aws_r5d_m5d_instances.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"bltbe833c29ff5eeb00\" alt=\"New AWS instances types in Elastic Cloud deployment templates\" \"=\"\">\n</p><figcaption>New AWS instances types in Elastic Cloud deployment templates</figcaption><p>These instance types offer improved disk performance via locally attached NVMe SSD. These drives offer higher throughput and lower latency when compared to Elastic Block Storage (EBS) General Purpose GP2 SSD volumes from the previous m5 and r4 instances. You can learn more about these new instances in our <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/blog/amazon-ec2-m5d-r5d-instances-on-elastic-cloud\">blog post</a>.\n</p> <strong><h2>Deploy anywhere</h2></strong><p>You can access Elastic Cloud in more regions. We recently added AWS Canada (Central), AWS EU (Paris), and AWS Asia Pacific (Seoul). But we aren’t just in the public cloud: Our self-managed options are designed to bring the cloud to you.\n</p> <strong><h3>Elastic Cloud Enterprise 2.6</h3></strong><p>We are pleased to announce the general availability of <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/ece\">Elastic Cloud Enterprise</a> (ECE) 2.6. ECE lets you centrally orchestrate a fleet of Elasticsearch clusters using the same software that Elastic uses to run Elastic Cloud. The latest version includes support for <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/enterprise-search\">Elastic Enterprise Search</a>, allowing you to create Workplace Search or App Search deployments on ECE.\n</p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt70c2a11dc62b6f02/5f36a899ddf9d7332963a0af/79blogece26enterprisesearch.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt70c2a11dc62b6f02\" alt=\"Enterprise Search deployment template on ECE\" \"=\"\">\n</p><figcaption>Elastic Enterprise Search deployment template on ECE</figcaption><p>ECE 2.6 also marks the general availability of the <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/ecctl/current/index.html\">Elastic Cloud Control</a> (ecctl) command-line interface (CLI). The CLI provides a unified experience for both ECE and Elastic Cloud’s API in a single tool. This offers a convenient alternative to programming language SDKs when using shell scripting, CI-CD tools, or infrastructure as code tools like Terraform. In addition to viewing and listing deployment templates, the release adds functionality to create deployments with command-line switches versus supplying a JSON file to the command.\n</p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt060f7218509c2568/5eb3256421a5027d7741f8d1/create_deployment_ecctl.gif\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt060f7218509c2568\" alt=\"ecctl command-line interface\" \"=\"\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto;\">\n</p><figcaption>ecctl command-line interface</figcaption><p>ECE 2.6 also inherits the in-place configuration changes introduced to Elastic Cloud in the same way just described, for faster and more reliable configuration updates.\n</p><h3>Elastic Cloud on Kubernetes 1.2</h3><p>We are also pleased to announce the general availability of Elastic Cloud on Kubernetes (ECK) 1.2. You can now use the Elastic Enterprise Search Custom Resource Definition (CRD), which makes launching an instance of App Search or Workplace Search simple.\n</p><!-- Put this wherever you would like your player to appear --><p><img class=\"vidyard-player-embed\" src=\"https://play.vidyard.com/v3x3tyPj7LVgbj8295G5Ty.jpg\" data-uuid=\"v3x3tyPj7LVgbj8295G5Ty\" data-v=\"4\" data-type=\"inline\" data-autoplay=\"1\" data-loop=\"1\" data-hidden_controls=\"1\" alt=\"Deploying enterprise search to Elastic Cloud on Kubernetes\" style=\"width: 100%; margin: auto; display: block;\">\n</p><figcaption>Deploying enterprise search to Elastic Cloud on Kubernetes</figcaption><p>The new 1.2 version also lets you take advantage of the new Beats Custom Resource Definition (CRD) to deploy and manage data shippers such as Filebeat, Metricbeat, Auditbeat, and others using ECK.&#160;\n</p><h2>Getting started</h2><p>Log in to the <a href=\"https://cloud.elastic.co/home\">Elastic Cloud console</a> to get started. If you’re new to Elastic Cloud, it’s easy to <a href=\"https://cloud.elastic.co/registration\">sign up for a free 14-day trial</a> with your Google Account and try it out.  If you are self-managing, download <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/downloads/enterprise\">ECE</a> or <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/downloads/elastic-cloud-kubernetes\">ECK</a> to get started on-premises or on your favorite IaaS provider.<br>\n</p>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Wed, 19 Aug 2020 16:02:00 +0000","feedId":7403,"bgimg":"https://play.vidyard.com/78P8dTvrtmDfLxvffaNaVK.jpg","linkMd5":"72ebad9ddcc132c907e0b9224b1a8dd9","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn4@2020_3/2020/08/24/23-30-23-355_3ed88704b5fe52a8.webp","destWidth":1280,"destHeight":720,"sourceBytes":35126,"destBytes":22950,"author":"Pieter Humphrey","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://play.vidyard.com/78P8dTvrtmDfLxvffaNaVK.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn4@2020_3/2020/08/24/23-30-23-355_3ed88704b5fe52a8.webp","https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/bltbf30048d22047193/5f3604e6c9ec57071816f7eb/Elastic-Cloud-Log-in-with-Google.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn23@2020_6/2020/08/24/23-30-47-219_991e67d73d6ebd4d.webp","https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/bltb6d134bfebc8ac60/5f361bd3578b8833eed82f46/subscription_change_elastic_cloud_console.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn20@2020_5/2020/08/24/23-30-46-037_a5f22f1b67f11633.webp","https://play.vidyard.com/PWmPzULwfWi9Wd4HXwipjv.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn57@2020_5/2020/08/24/23-30-46-072_6078fdc66d6a317c.webp","https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/bltbe833c29ff5eeb00/5f36a86a7ca22b018aed979c/elastic_cloud_aws_r5d_m5d_instances.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn47@2020_1/2020/08/24/23-30-45-907_d045b547c2300d96.webp","https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt70c2a11dc62b6f02/5f36a899ddf9d7332963a0af/79blogece26enterprisesearch.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn75@2020_3/2020/08/24/23-30-46-028_e3c5b39fde4894f2.webp","https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt060f7218509c2568/5eb3256421a5027d7741f8d1/create_deployment_ecctl.gif":null,"https://play.vidyard.com/v3x3tyPj7LVgbj8295G5Ty.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn90@2020_2/2020/08/24/23-30-46-704_afe975fb8c385e4b.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598311822916},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 07:30:22","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 07:30:22","title":"Find strings within strings faster with the new wildcard field","link":"https://www.elastic.co/blog/find-strings-within-strings-faster-with-the-new-elasticsearch-wildcard-field","description":"<p>In Elasticsearch 7.9, we’ll be introducing a new “wildcard” field type optimised for quickly finding patterns inside string values. This new field type addresses best practices for efficiently indexing and <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/log-monitoring\">searching within logs</a> and <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/security\">security data</a> by taking a whole new approach to how we index string data. Depending on your existing field usage, wildcards can provide: </p>\n<ul> \n <li>Simpler search expressions (no need to AND words/phrases together)</li> \n <li>Simpler indexing (no need to define choices of analyzers)</li> \n <li>No more missing values</li> \n <li>Faster search</li> \n <li>Less disk usage</li> \n</ul>\n<p>The most exciting feature of this new data type is its simplification of partial matches. With wildcards, you no longer need to worry about where your text pattern falls within a string. Just search using normal query syntax, and Elasticsearch will find all matches anywhere in a string. That's right, we did this all <strong>without requiring you to change your query syntax</strong>. </p>\n<p>The wildcard data type addresses issues <a href=\"https://socprime.com/blog/elastic-for-security-analysts-part-1-searching-strings/\">noted by those in the security field</a> when it comes to partial matching of string values, but has applications in <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/observability\">observability</a> and beyond (covered below). In particular, users wanting to search machine-generated logs should find this new field type a more natural fit than existing options. </p>\n<p>In this blog, we'll take a look at how we've been able to accomplish this, as well as how you'll be able to best benefit from wildcard fields. Let's take a look. </p>\n<h2>Existing options for indexing strings</h2>\n<p>Until 7.9, when it came to searching strings, Elasticsearch has broadly offered two choices: </p>\n<ol> \n <li><code>Text</code> fields</li> \n <li><code>Keyword</code> fields</li> \n</ol>\n<p><code>Text</code> fields “<a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/elasticsearch/reference/7.9/analysis-tokenizers.html\">tokenize</a>” strings into multiple tokens, each typically representing words. Searchers looking for <em>quick foxes</em> can therefore match articles talking about a <em>quick brown fox</em>. </p>\n<p><code>Keyword</code> fields tend to be used for shorter, structured content like a country code which would be indexed without analysis as a single token. These can then be used by analysts e.g. to visualize popular holiday destinations using <em>aggregations</em>. </p>\n<p>Without an explicit mapping choice by users, Elasticsearch’s default index template assumes that string values presented in JSON documents should be indexed as both a <strong>text</strong> field and a <strong>keyword </strong>field. The intention is that Elasticsearch should work if the string represents prose that should be chopped into multiple word tokens for search <em>or</em> if it represents a single structured value (like the city name “New York”) that should be displayed as one item when displayed in a “top travel destinations” bar chart. </p>\n<h2>When text and keyword fields fall short</h2>\n<p>We don’t have to look very far for an example of string content that doesn’t work well as either text or keyword fields. Elasticsearch’s own log files produce messages that don’t fit neatly into either category. Let’s examine why. </p>\n<p>Each Elasticsearch log file entry follows the classic minimal requirement for a logged event - a timestamp and a message. The messages that are logged are used to describe a variety of events in a semi-structured form e.g.: </p>\n<ol> \n <li>Indexes being created or deleted</li> \n <li>Servers joining or leaving the cluster</li> \n <li>Warnings of resources under pressure</li> \n <li>Detailed error messages with long stack traces</li> \n</ol>\n<p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/bltca07898874ebd078/5f3707a6db5c28785b6f041b/blog-wildcard-field-stack-trace.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"bltca07898874ebd078\" alt=\"Elasticsearch stack trace\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto; width:600px;\"> </img></p>\n<p>Above is an example of a logged event. Highlighted is a section of the message that I’d like to search for to find similar logged errors. That would be a simple “Cmd/Ctrl-F” key press in a text editor, but is not quite so simple using Elasticsearch. Remember, Elasticsearch operates <em>at scale</em>, so Ctrl-F is great for a small text file, but not for TBs of logs. An index of some kind is required to make searches fast. </p>\n<h4>Log message as text field</h4>\n<p>If we indexed the message content as a <strong>text</strong> field the end user immediately has a translation problem. The selected substring is “<code>NodeNotConnectedException:[54b_data_2]</code>” but the critical question is “how this is represented in the index”? </p>\n<ul> \n <li>Is it half a token?</li> \n <li>One token?</li> \n <li>One and a half tokens?</li> \n <li>Two? Two and a half? ...</li> \n</ul>\n<p>The mystery arises because, unlike English text such as “<code>quick brown fox</code>”, we have no clue where words begin or end in log messages like the stack trace shown above. This is not a language humans speak naturally so it is easy to accidentally select only part of a word. </p>\n<p>The <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/elasticsearch/reference/7.9/query-dsl-match-query.html\">match</a> query can help users avoid having to know if the : or [ or _ characters in our selection are parts of word tokens or just used to split words. However, the match query will <em>not</em> help with knowing if the beginning or end of our selection happens to crop what would otherwise be a complete token. If the choice of <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/elasticsearch/reference/7.9/configure-text-analysis.html\">analyzer</a> kept the full stops in the Java package names we would have to use an expensive leading wildcard search for <code>*NodeNotConnectedException</code> or back-up the text selection to add the preceding <code>org.elasticsearch.transport.</code> characters in our “match” query. </p>\n<p>Constructing a query that is respectful of tokenisation policies is a significant challenge, even for experienced Elasticsearch developers. </p>\n<h4>Log message as a keyword field</h4>\n<p>The keyword field has much less mystery about how the indexed content is stored — it’s one string and we can use a single regexp or wildcard expression to match arbitrary fragments like our search string inside the field values. </p>\n<p>Search is made much simpler for users but there are two main issues with the execution: </p>\n<ol> \n <li>Search speed</li> \n <li>Missing data</li> \n</ol>\n<p>Search speeds are very slow if, like our search, we are searching for something in the middle of strings and there are many unique values. Although keyword fields are indexed, it’s the wrong data structure for this task. An equivalent task would be a researcher trying to find all words with “oo” in the dictionary. They’d have to scan every word on every page from A to Z to find values “afoot” to “zoo”. That’s essentially what the keyword field has to do with every unique value and the index is of no help in speeding this up. Making things more difficult, log messages often produce many unique string values if, like those produced by Elasticsearch, they contain variables such as timings and memory sizes etc. </p>\n<p>The other big issue with the keyword field is it can’t handle very long fields. The default string mapping ignores strings longer than 256 characters, <a href=\"https://discuss.elastic.co/t/i-need-help-elasticsearch-does-not-match-my-regexp-wildcard-or-query-string-queries/239473\">silently dropping values from the list of indexed terms</a>. The majority of Elasticsearch’s log file messages exceed this limit. </p>\n<p>And even if you <em>do</em> <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/elasticsearch/reference/7.9/ignore-above.html\">raise</a> the Elasticsearch limit, you cannot exceed the hard Lucene limit of 32k for a single token, and Elasticsearch certainly logs some messages that exceed this. </p>\n<p>This makes a bit of a blind spot for our logging which is undesirable and, in some security cases, unacceptable. </p>\n<h2>Introducing the new wildcard field</h2>\n<p>To solve these issues we have a new <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/elasticsearch/reference/7.9/wildcard.html\">wildcard field</a>&nbsp;which is adept at finding any patterns in the middle of arbitrary string values. </p>\n<ul> \n <li>Unlike the text field, it does <strong>not</strong> treat strings as a collection of words separated by punctuation.</li> \n <li>Unlike the keyword field, it can be very fast to infix search many unique values and is free of size restrictions.</li> \n</ul>\n<h3>How it works</h3>\n<p>Detailed pattern-matching operations like wildcard or regular expressions can be expensive to run on large data sets. The key to speeding up these operations is to limit the amount of data on which you apply these detailed comparisons. If a quick <em>approximate</em> match can be applied to narrow the bulk of the data then the more expensive operation of detailed matching can be reserved to verifying a few promising candidate matches. </p>\n<p>The new wildcard field automatically accelerates wildcard and regexp searches in this way using two data structures: </p>\n<ol> \n <li>An “n-gram” index of all 3 character sequences found in strings</li> \n <li>A “binary doc value” store of the full original document values</li> \n</ol>\n<p>The first structure is used to provide a fast but rough narrowing of candidates based on matching n-gram fragments from the search string. </p>\n<p>The second data structure is used to verify the match candidates produced by the n-gram match with an automaton query. </p>\n<h4>Wildcard example</h4>\n<p>A security analyst might be interested in finding any log messages where a hacker may have referred to the word “shell”. Given the wildcard query “<strong>*shell*</strong>” the wildcard field would automatically split this search string into 3 character n-grams, creating the equivalent of this Lucene query: </p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 20px;\"><code>she AND ell</code> </p>\n<p>This would reduce the number of documents we need to consider but may produce some false positives — e.g. a document containing the string </p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 20px;\"><code>/Users/Me/Documents/F</code><strong><code>ell</code></strong><code> walking trip with </code><strong><code>She</code></strong><code>ila.docx</code> </p>\n<p>Obviously, the longer the search string the fewer false positives produced. A search for “<code>*powershell.exe*</code>”, for example would produce a much more selective n-gram query of </p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 20px;\"><code>pow AND wer AND rsh AND hel AND l.e AND exe</code> </p>\n<p>Removing any false positives is a necessary finishing stage and so the wildcard field does this check on all the matches produced by the rough n-gram query. This is done by retrieving the full original value from a Lucene binary doc value store and running the wildcard or regexp pattern on the full value. </p>\n<h4>Regexp example</h4>\n<p>Regular expressions are more complex and can include choices of value paths&nbsp;— e.g. this search for references to DLL or EXE files: </p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 20px;\"><code>.*\\.(dll|exe)</code> </p>\n<p>To accelerate this sort of query the expression is parsed into n-grams arranged with this Boolean logic: </p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 20px;\"><code> (.dl AND ll_) OR (.ex AND xe_)</code> </p>\n<p>Note in this example that the _ character is used to show where a non-printable character is added to mark the end of the indexed string. </p> \n<h4>Regular expressions and case sensitivity</h4>\n<p>We know security analysts have <a href=\"https://socprime.com/blog/elastic-for-security-analysts-part-1-searching-strings/\">adapted</a> their Elasticsearch regexp searches to use mixed case expressions e.g when searching for references to <code>cmd.exe</code> regardless of case they will write: </p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 20px;\"><code> [Cc][Mm][Dd].[Ee][Xx][Ee] </code> </p>\n<p>The wildcard field recognises these mixed case expressions and the rough-cut query for the above expression would be optimised to the equivalent of this Lucene query: </p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 20px;\"><code>cmd AND d.e AND exe</code> </p>\n<p>(Note the n-gram index used to accelerate queries is always lowercased). </p>\n<p>Existing security rules that use these mixed case expressions on keyword fields should run faster when used on content indexed into a wildcard field. </p>\n<p>However, these expressions still look ugly and are a pain to write, so in a future Elasticsearch release we also hope to introduce a <a href=\"https://github.com/elastic/elasticsearch/pull/59441\">case sensitive flag</a> to the regexp query which can be set to “false” to make a query case insensitive. It will essentially make a search for <em>abc</em> search for <code>[Aa][Bb][Cc]</code> without the need for the user to write all those square brackets. The net result will be simpler, faster rules. </p>\n<h2>Comparison with keyword fields</h2>\n<p>One key goal of the wildcard field is to be a drop-in replacement for keyword fields where it makes sense. We have worked hard to ensure that it produces the same results for all query types — albeit sometimes wildcard will be faster and sometimes slower. This table should help compare the two fields: </p>\n<table style=\"width:100%\"> \n <tbody> \n  <tr style=\"background-color:#E8F6FE;\"> \n   <td style=\"font-weight:700;\">Feature </td> \n   <td style=\"font-weight:700;\">Keyword </td> \n   <td style=\"font-weight:700;\">Wildcard </td> \n  </tr> \n  <tr> \n   <td>Sorting speeds </td> \n   <td>Fast </td> \n   <td>Not quite as fast<em> (see *1)</em> </td> \n  </tr> \n  <tr style=\"background-color:#F6F7FA;\"> \n   <td>Aggregation speeds </td> \n   <td>Fast </td> \n   <td>Not quite as fast <em>(see *1)</em> </td> \n  </tr> \n  <tr> \n   <td>Prefix query speeds (foo*) </td> \n   <td>Fast </td> \n   <td>Not quite as fast<em> (see *2)</em> </td> \n  </tr> \n  <tr style=\"background-color:#F6F7FA;\"> \n   <td>Leading wildcard query speeds on low-cardinality fields (*foo) </td> \n   <td>Fast </td> \n   <td>Slower <em>(see *3)</em> </td> \n  </tr> \n  <tr> \n   <td>Leading wildcard or regexp&nbsp;query speeds on high-cardinality fields (*foo) </td> \n   <td>Terrible </td> \n   <td>Much faster </td> \n  </tr> \n  <tr style=\"background-color:#F6F7FA;\"> \n   <td>Term query. full value match (foo) </td> \n   <td>Fast </td> \n   <td>Not quite as fast<em> (see *2)</em> </td> \n  </tr> \n  <tr> \n   <td>Fuzzy query</td> \n   <td>Y <em>(see *4)</em> </td> \n   <td>Y </td> \n  </tr> \n  <tr style=\"background-color:#F6F7FA;\"> \n   <td>Regexp query</td> \n   <td>Y <em>(see *4)</em> </td> \n   <td>Y </td> \n  </tr> \n  <tr> \n   <td>Range query</td> \n   <td>Y <em>(see *4)</em> </td> \n   <td>Y </td> \n  </tr> \n  <tr style=\"background-color:#F6F7FA;\"> \n   <td>Supports highlighting </td> \n   <td>Y </td> \n   <td>N </td> \n  </tr> \n  <tr> \n   <td>Searched by \"all field\" queries </td> \n   <td>Y </td> \n   <td>Y </td> \n  </tr> \n  <tr style=\"background-color:#F6F7FA;\"> \n   <td>Disk costs for mostly unique values </td> \n   <td>high <em>(see *5)</em> </td> \n   <td>lower <em>(see *5)</em> </td> \n  </tr> \n  <tr> \n   <td>Disk costs for mostly identical values </td> \n   <td>low <em>(see *5)</em> </td> \n   <td>medium <em>(see *5)</em> </td> \n  </tr> \n  <tr style=\"background-color:#F6F7FA;\"> \n   <td>Max character size for a field value </td> \n   <td>256 for default JSON string mappings, 32,766 Lucene max </td> \n   <td>unlimited </td> \n  </tr> \n  <tr> \n   <td>Supports normalizers in mappings </td> \n   <td>Y </td> \n   <td>N </td> \n  </tr> \n  <tr style=\"background-color:#F6F7FA;\"> \n   <td>Indexing speeds </td> \n   <td>Fast </td> \n   <td>Slower <em>(see *6)</em> </td> \n  </tr> \n </tbody> \n</table>\n<ol> \n <li>Somewhat slower as doc values retrieved from compressed blocks of 32</li> \n <li>Somewhat slower because approximate matches with n-grams need verification</li> \n <li>Keyword field visits every unique value only once but wildcard field assesses every utterance of values</li> \n <li>If “allow expensive queries” setting enabled</li> \n <li>It depends on common prefixes — keyword fields have common-prefix based compression whereas wildcard fields are whole-value LZ4 compression</li> \n <li>Will vary with content but a test indexing weblogs took 499 seconds Vs keyword’s 365 seconds</li> \n</ol>\n<h3>What’s next?</h3>\n<p>To make life easier for search and analytics applications contemplating the growing list of field types, we have simplified the way indexes describe fields in the field capabilities API. Physical fields like <code>wildcard</code> and <code>constant_keyword</code> that are functionally equivalent to the <code>keyword</code> field now describe themselves as belonging to the <code>keyword</code> <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/elasticsearch/reference/7.9/search-field-caps.html#search-field-caps-api-response-body\">family</a>. The introduction of <em>family</em> types mean other field type groupings can be made in the future and adopted without downstream client applications needing to know about new physical field types. As long as they behave the same as other members of the same family they can be used without any changes to the client application. </p>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/blog/introducing-the-elastic-common-schema\">Elastic Common Schema</a> (ECS) defines standardised index mappings for popular data fields. </p>\n<p>In 7.9, ECS has not been updated to use wildcard fields but in the future we can expect that several high-cardinality keyword fields will be changed over to use wildcard fields instead. When this happens client applications should typically not need to change anything as a result but should benefit from faster searches. </p>\n<h2>All sounds great, what does it cost me?</h2>\n<p>As usual, the answer is “it depends”. Storage costs mostly depend on the level of duplication in your field’s data. For mostly-unique values the storage costs can actually be less than those of using a keyword field. This is because we made an <a href=\"https://issues.apache.org/jira/browse/LUCENE-9211\">addition</a> to Lucene to compress the binary doc value store used to access the original content. </p>\n<p>We tested an example dataset of 2 million weblog records where each line in the file is indexed as a single value held in a single field. An index using a keyword field was 310MB but thanks to compression the equivalent index using the new wildcard field was 227MB. Admittedly the timestamp helped ensure each document value was unique but combinations of URL, IP address and user-agent could also lead to many unique strings. </p>\n<h2>Summary cheat sheet — when should I use a wildcard field?</h2>\n<p>We recognise all of the above is a lot to think about so a rough guide to when a wildcard field is appropriate is given below. </p>\n<p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt086af7a2897168a8/5f37050cdb5c28785b6f0413/blog-wildcard-field-workflow.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt086af7a2897168a8\" alt=\"When and when not to use a wildcard field\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto;\"> </img></p>\n<h2>Take it for a spin</h2>\n<p>As someone who’s helped develop this feature, I’m excited to see it production-ready. Wildcard fields can provide an immediate boost for almost every Elasticsearch user, not just folks in the <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/security\">security</a> and <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/observability\">observability</a> spaces. I know I’ll be using it to search my own Elasticsearch log files. </p>\n<p>If all this has piqued your interest, try wildcards out for yourself in a <a href=\"https://cloud.elastic.co/registration?elektra=blog-wildcardfields\">free trial of Elasticsearch Service</a>, and let us know what you think in our <a href=\"https://discuss.elastic.co\">forums</a>.
