Once you click “Create deployment,” your Elastic Cloud workload is provisioned in minutes.\n</p><p>Visit our <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/cloud/current/index.html?blade=cloud.elastic.co\">documentation</a> to learn more.\n</p><h2>Elastic Cloud and marketplace integration\n</h2><p>If you’re an AWS customer, you can also subscribe to Elastic Cloud and Elasticsearch Service via the <a href=\"https://aws.amazon.com/marketplace/pp/B01N6YCISK?qid=1571899641796&sr=0-1&ref_=srh_res_product_title\">AWS Marketplace</a>. You can select between usage plans, and integrated billing consolidates your Elastic Cloud usage charges into your existing AWS bill. These charges are deductible from your prepaid AWS Enterprise Discount Program agreements.\n</p><p>Getting started is easy: Log in to the <a href=\"https://cloud.elastic.co/home\">Elastic Cloud console</a> or <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/aws\">sign up for a free 14-day trial</a>.\n</p><p><br></p>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Mon, 17 Aug 2020 15:00:00 +0000","feedId":7403,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"29f721f63dfa34f25959ca2724ba0b37","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"The Elastic Cloud Team","publishedOrCreatedDate":1598311822917},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 07:30:22","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 07:30:22","title":"Threat hunting capture the flag with Elastic Security: BSides 2020","link":"https://www.elastic.co/blog/threat-hunting-capture-the-flag-elastic-security-bsides-2020","description":"<p>Last month, members of the <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/security\">Elastic Security</a> team hosted a <a href=\"https://community.elastic.co/events/details/elastic-amer-virtual-presents-bsides-satx-2020-threat-hunting-ctf-with-elastic/\">threat hunting capture the flag (CTF)</a> event at BSides SATX. We provided the community with an environment to learn and practice threat hunting with our team, and cultivated new relationships with attendees. By sharing information with security practitioners, we can help prepare them to defend their organization’s data from attack through knowledge transfer. Our free and open approach to security includes not only technologies, but our philosophy and methodologies around threat hunting based on the research and content that we also share. </p>\n<p>Like many other conferences this year, <a href=\"https://www.bsidessatx.com/\">BSides SATX</a> shifted to a virtual format in light of the global COVID-19 pandemic. The conference was a great experience thanks to the organizers and everyone who participated. Attendees utilized our CTF environment, built with free and open technologies, before learning how to use version 7.8 of the Elastic Stack to hunt for an attacker’s malicious behavior in the proverbial <em>needle in a haystack</em>: 11 million events. </p>\n<h2>The scenario</h2>\n<p>At the beginning of the CTF competition, participants were provided with an all-too-common scenario that included a notification from a business partner. Their network had been compromised and some endpoints were infected with ransomware. They recovered from the incident and offered their assurances that “you shouldn’t be affected.” However, in this scenario, leadership is skeptical and has asked attendees&nbsp; — the participating threat hunters — to look for any suspicious behavior in their environment. After all, the third party has a valid user account for systems in the enterprise that they use occasionally to connect to your network… </p>\n<p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt01c85624c58d522b/5f31cb38327a6201d7ebaa0a/1-fictitious-breach-blog-capture-the-flag.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt01c85624c58d522b\" alt=\"\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto; border:1px solid black; width: 90%\"> </img></p>\n<figcaption>\n Figure 1 - An excerpt from the fictitious breach notification from a business partner\n</figcaption>\n<h2>Threat hunting methodology</h2>\n<p>Threat hunting is the <em>proactive</em> pursuit and elimination of adversaries in an organization’s environment before they cause damage and loss. In the spirit of threat hunting, we did not generate any security alerts for participants before the CTF event. After providing attendees with a demonstration of how to use the Discover and Visualize apps in <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/kibana\">Kibana</a> and execute queries using <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/kibana/7.8/kuery-query.html\">Kibana Query Language (KQL)</a>, we provided them with the fabricated breach notification. As threat hunters, they would need to form hypotheses about which actions the attacker may have taken before searching for the behavior. </p>\n<p>An initial hypothesis that could be quickly proved or disproved may be something like: “the attacker abused the valid user account used by the business partner by authenticating to one or more of the endpoints in my network.” If the hypothesis was proven to be true, an additional hypothesis could be formed based on assumptions on what the attacker could have done next. Did they perform any actions to discover the network? Did they establish a command and control channel or exfiltrate any data? </p>\n<p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt976e705549be29cf/5f31cb4b48d3b57cb974969b/2-visualize-in-kibana-blog-capture-the-flag.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt976e705549be29cf\" alt=\"\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto; border:1px solid black\"> </img></p>\n<figcaption>\n Figure 2 - Using Visualize in Kibana to search for rare process command line arguments \n</figcaption>\n<p>Once CTF participants discovered the first signs of malicious activity, they began to track the adversary’s footprints through the data and untangle the story of what happened piece by piece. </p>\n<p>If you’re interested in learning more about the fundamentals of effective threat hunting, you can download our free book, <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/campaigns/elastic-guide-to-threat-hunting\">The Elastic Guide to Threat Hunting</a> and watch our presentation, <a href=\"https://youtu.be/2Hh5spqA6bw\">How to plan and execute a hunt</a>. </p>\n<p><br> </br></p>\n<div class=\"video embed-container\" style=\"height: 319.725px;\"> \n <img class=\"vidyard-player-embed\" src=\"https://play.vidyard.com/8PKneMzyB45NAMP7dBxzYm.jpg\" data-uuid=\"8PKneMzyB45NAMP7dBxzYm\" data-v=\"4\" data-type=\"inline\" style=\"width: 100%; margin: auto; display: block;\"> </img>\n</div>\n<p><strong></strong> </p>\n<h2>Preparing the CTF environment</h2>\n<p>While designing the environment for the CTF, we decided to keep it relatively simple. We configured the following components, as shown in Figure 3: </p>\n<ul> \n <li>x1 Windows Server 2016 host configured as a domain controller</li> \n <li>x20 Windows 10 endpoints joined to the domain</li> \n <li>We installed several applications on each endpoint using <a href=\"https://ninite.com/\">Ninite</a> in order to generate benign events and make the task of finding the flags in the dataset a bit more challenging for participants — after all, the goal of a threat hunter is to find the true threat amidst the noise</li> \n <li><a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/beats/winlogbeat\">Winlogbeat</a> was installed on all endpoints and configured to ship Windows event logs, PowerShell logs, and <a href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/sysinternals/downloads/sysmon\">Sysmon</a> events to <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/elastic-stack\">Elasticsearch</a>. We used Olaf Hartong's popular <a href=\"https://github.com/olafhartong/sysmon-modular\">sysmon-modular</a> configuration with Sysmon</li> \n <li><a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/beats/packetbeat\">Packetbeat</a> was installed on all endpoints to ship network events to Elasticsearch</li> \n <li>All events were shipped to Elasticsearch running in an <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/cloud/\">Elastic Cloud</a> cluster</li> \n <li>Participants utilized <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/kibana\">Kibana</a> to search and visualize the events indexed in Elasticsearch</li> \n <li>A <a href=\"https://ctfd.io/\">CTFd</a> platform was setup for participants to submit flags and score points during the event</li> \n</ul>\n<p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt4483b0dc55e52ffb/5f31cb5f7ca22b018aed84b4/3-configure-CTF-range-blog-capture-the-flag.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt4483b0dc55e52ffb\" alt=\"\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto; border:1px solid black\"> </img></p>\n<figcaption>\n Figure 3 - Overview of how we configured our CTF range \n</figcaption>\n<p>Once the CTF environment was configured and the logs were flowing to Elasticsearch, it was time to simulate the malicious activity of our threat actor as they worked to achieve their mission: stealing sensitive data from their target. Our team executed offensive techniques across the <a href=\"https://attack.mitre.org/\">MITRE ATT&amp;CK®</a> matrix in order to simulate attacker activity and achieve their objective. </p>\n<p>Attacker techniques were executed using both <a href=\"https://github.com/LOLBAS-Project/LOLBAS\">living-off-the-land</a> methods and <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/blog/embracing-offensive-tooling-building-detections-against-koadic-using-eql\">off-the-shelf offensive tools</a> to complete objectives such as obtaining initial access to an endpoint, establishing a command and control channel, maintaining persistence, and moving laterally between endpoints. CTF participants were asked to hunt for evidence of the nefarious activity without many leads and earn points for submitting the correct flags. The winner was the first person to submit all of the correct flags. </p>\n<p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt862241dc6591d60b/5f31cb6e7f3b7908db0322b0/4-ctfd-scoreboard-capture-the-flag.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt862241dc6591d60b\" alt=\"\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto; border:1px solid black\"> </img></p>\n<figcaption>\n Figure 4 - Our CTFd scoreboard \n</figcaption>\n<p>For the benefit of people who had not used Kibana to execute queries or build visualizations before, we provided them with a brief demonstration and links to resources to jump start their threat hunting efforts. We saw a few people who were new to threat hunting with Elastic arrive on the leaderboard early on. We received positive feedback from attendees, including someone who was new to Elastic and was looking to sharpen their skills before starting a new job the following week. Congratulations again to the winner, <a href=\"https://twitter.com/theparanoidnerd\">@theparanoidnerd</a>! </p>\n<p><br> </br></p> \n<h2>How Elastic enables security teams</h2>\n<p>A successful security team has implemented human processes that are aided by technology. Whether a security practitioner belongs to a threat hunting, security operations, or incident response team, they must be able to rely on both their tools and the <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Data_quality\">quality</a> of their data. </p>\n<h3>Ensuring data quality with Beats and Elastic Common Schema</h3>\n<p>To help ensure security teams have the right data available to them in Elastic Security for monitoring and detection, we have <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/beats/filebeat/7.8/filebeat-modules.html\">numerous </a>Beats that can collect, normalize, and ship data directly to Elasticsearch. Each Beat comes with a variety of modules that will create the index mappings and predefined dashboards, specific to the log source. </p>\n<p>A security team must be able to rely on the accuracy and timeliness of their data. If you don’t know which systems have logging and event forwarding enabled, which fields are available in each log and the expected data types or values for those fields, or if the schema for a data source changes unexpectedly, you cannot rely on the results of your queries. </p>\n<p>The <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/ecs/current/index.html\">Elastic Common Schema (ECS)</a> defines a common set of fields to be used when storing event data in Elasticsearch, such as logs and metrics. This may sound simple at first, but taking on the task of extracting, transforming, and loading events from multiple sources into a single destination can be a costly, time consuming, and continuous effort. Since version 7.0 of our Beats framework, the normalization of data to ECS is taken care of for you, ensuring consistency across your data and helping users execute searches effectively. </p>\n<figcaption>\n <img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/bltaa232bbebf5385b1/5f32c8787ca22b018aed893b/5-ECS-fields-blog-capture-the-flag-2.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"bltaa232bbebf5385b1\" alt=\"\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto; border:1px solid black; width: 55%\" />\n</figcaption>\n<figcaption>\n Figure 5 - ECS fields shown in Kibana Discover along with event filter options \n</figcaption>\n<p>ECS also adds <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/ecs/current/ecs-category-field-values-reference.html\">categorization fields</a> to each event, allowing threat hunters to describe the data they are looking for as opposed to querying for the specific event information. For example, Windows event ID 4624 records a “Successful Logon” event. With ECS we can find it using the query <code>event.category: authentication and event.outcome: success</code>. This allows a threat hunter to search across multiple data sources without having to be concerned about the specific fields and values in each log. </p>\n<figcaption>\n <img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/bltebda99de5a7b19e6/5f32c94c8e1e8d4046e9f300/6-event-category-field-blog-capture-the-flag-2.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"bltebda99de5a7b19e6\" alt=\"\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto; border:1px solid black; width: 55%\"><br>Figure 6 - Example values for the event.category field </br></img>\n</figcaption>\n<h3>Schema on write and for faster search results</h3>\n<p>Elastic uses <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/blog/schema-on-write-vs-schema-on-read\">schema on write</a> to extract the necessary fields from logs <em>prior </em>to storing them. With schema on write, analysts do not have to figure out how to extract fields after the fact, speeding up their queries, and dramatically increasing the value they get from their log data. </p>\n<p>Elastic takes care of the <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/elasticsearch/reference/7.8/mapping-types.html\">mapping</a> of data types to the various fields in an event. This allows for additional functionality when searching. For example, by mapping the <code>source.bytes</code> field as a <code>long</code> data type, threat hunters can use a range of numbers as opposed to a single number. If <code>source.bytes</code> was mapped to the <code>text</code> or <code>keyword</code> data type, it could not be used the same way. </p>\n<p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt1f8964774e3b49df/5f31cb9f168ec908bebe96cb/7-winlogbeat-blog-capture-the-flag.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt1f8964774e3b49df\" alt=\"\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto; border:1px solid black\"> </img></p>\n<figcaption>\n Figure 7 - Reviewing field mappings for the winlogbeat index \n</figcaption>\n<p>Using schema on write also allows for the enriching of data with other information such as indicator of compromise (IOC) matches, user information such as department and role, and the risk level of an asset. When events are enriched, it’s easy to search for the relevant fields and help with an analyst’s situational awareness. </p>\n<p>Figure 8 shows how <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/kibana/7.8/discover.html\">Discover</a> helps users construct search queries by utilizing KQL’s autocomplete feature to suggest valid field names. </p>\n<p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt488cb414ba2c0a3d/5f31cbb18e1e8d4046e9eec9/8-kql-autocomplete-blog-capture-the-flag.gif\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt488cb414ba2c0a3d\" alt=\"\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto; border:1px solid black; width: 90%\"> </img></p>\n<figcaption>\n Figure 8 - Using KQL’s autocomplete feature \n</figcaption>\n<p>After choosing a field to include in a query, KQL’s autocomplete asks the user what operator they would like to use with the field, based on its <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/elasticsearch/reference/7.8/mapping-types.html\">data type mapping</a>. For example, a numeric field type like <code>source.bytes</code> can be used with the operators, as shown in Figure 9 below. </p>\n<img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt317c1d68ac8d77a5/5f31cbbec9ec57071816e76b/9-numeric-field-types-blog-capture-the-flag.jpg\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt317c1d68ac8d77a5\" alt=\"\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto; border:1px solid black; width: 90%\">&nbsp;\n <figcaption>\n  Figure 9 - List of operators that can be used with the numeric field type, source.bytes \n </figcaption><h2>Detecting malicious behavior with Elastic Security</h2><p>Elastic Security builds on the power of the Elastic Stack to deliver out-of-the-box capabilities that help security operations teams do their jobs more effectively. For example, Elastic Security has 140+ prebuilt <a href=\"https://github.com/elastic/detection-rules\">detection rules</a> to alert on attacker behavior on endpoint, network, and cloud platforms. All detections are mapped to the relevant ATT&amp;CK techniques and include useful references to help analysts triage and investigate alerts. Users also have the option to create their own rules and we are always excited to work alongside members of the community to help with and accept their <a href=\"https://github.com/elastic/detection-rules/blob/main/CONTRIBUTING.md\">contributions</a>. </p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/bltf4319b5921db4cb3/5f31cbc9c9ec57071816e76f/10-alerts-detection-rules-blog-capture-the-flag.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"bltf4319b5921db4cb3\" alt=\"\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto; border:1px solid black\"> </img></p>\n <figcaption>\n  Figure 10 - Alerts generated by detection rules in Elastic Security \n </figcaption><p>Analysts can drag and drop fields of interest into the Timeline for further investigation. Data from multiple indices, or data sources, can be added to Timeline, which enables analysts to organize leads and investigate complex threats. </p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt35f269fc1ab7657a/5f31cbd69af97004e7f63d58/11-timeline-blog-capture-the-flag.gif\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt35f269fc1ab7657a\" alt=\"\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto; border:1px solid black\"> </img></p>\n <figcaption>\n  Figure 11 - Using Timeline in Elastic Security to investigate schtasks.exe creation events \n </figcaption><p> <strong></strong> </p><h2>Conclusion</h2><p>In this post, we shared some details about the threat hunting CTF event that Elastic Security delivered for <a href=\"https://twitter.com/BSidesSATX\">BSides SATX</a> using free and open technologies. Thank you to all of the BSides organizers and participants that made the virtual conference a success. </p><p>We also discussed how the Elastic Stack enables threat hunting and how Elastic Security can help security operations teams with their monitoring and detection efforts to protect their organization’s data from attack. </p><p>If you’re interested in learning more about Elastic Security, you can <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/security\">download for free</a> or sign up for a free 14-day trial of <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/cloud/\">Elastic Cloud</a>. 07:30:22","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 07:30:22","title":"Elastic at KubeCon Europe 2020: Orchestration to observability, and beyond!","link":"https://www.elastic.co/blog/elastic-at-kubecon-europe-2020-orchestration-to-observability-and-beyond","description":"<p>KubeCon Europe 2020 is virtual this year, and Elastic is doing our part to help \"keep cloud native connected.\" We would rather be there in person to shake hands, tell stories, and laugh, but the challenges of a virtual conference also provide the opportunity to share great content and materials that we might not be able to at a crowded booth.\n</p><p>So please join us at our virtual booth at Virtual KubeCon 2020 (Amsterdam) to see a virtual demo session, get access to custom content, grab exclusive virtual swag, and chat with real people.\n</p><p>At the booth we’ll give a quick introduction to Elasticsearch (the <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/blog/elasticsearch-free-open-limitless\">free and open</a>, scalable, and highly-available search engine and datastore) and show how it powers use cases like enterprise search, observability, and security. We’ll demonstrate how the applications that power Elastic Observability (Logs, Metrics, APM, and Uptime) consolidate the event streams from your Kubernetes cluster, cloud and physical infrastructure, Prometheus, and other CNCF projects and services with your application traces in a fast, scalable, and unified data store. We'll also show you how to spin up an Elastic Stack cluster with Elastic Cloud on Kubernetes (ECK), the official operator for orchestrating Elasticsearch on Kubernetes.\n</p><h2>Orchestration with Elastic Cloud on Kubernetes</h2><p>At KubeCon 2019 in San Diego, the buzz was about the beta of Elastic Cloud on Kubernetes (ECK). ECK is the official operator for orchestration of Elasticsearch and the Elastic Stack — not just for initial day-one deployments, but also for day-two administration and beyond.&#160;&#160;\n</p><p>This year, we’re excited to chat with folks who have been using ECK to simplify the orchestration of their Elastic Stack deployments and get feedback around how we can continue to improve their experience. The ECK operator is now generally available (GA) and <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/cloud-on-k8s/current/release-notes-1.2.0.html\">was recently updated</a> to include the ability to orchestrate the lifecycle for Elastic Enterprise Search as well as Beats. Deploying an Elastic cluster with ECK is as simple as installing the operator: \n</p><pre class=\"prettyprint\">kubectl apply -f https://download.elastic.co/downloads/eck/1.1.2/all-in-one.yaml</pre> <p>Then applying a configuration, as shown in this example, to create a three-node cluster:</p><pre class=\"prettyprint\">cat &#60;&#60;EOF | kubectl apply -f - \napiVersion: elasticsearch.k8s.elastic.co/v1 \nkind: Elasticsearch \nmetadata: \n  name: quickstart \nspec: \n  version: 7.8.0 \n  nodeSets: \n  - name: default \n    count: 3 \n    config: \n      node.master: true \n      node.data: true \n      node.ingest: true \n      node.store.allow_mmap: false \nEOF\n</pre><p>ECK can streamline the orchestration of your Elastic deployments when running on Kubernetes, but when using the Elastic Stack you have more than one choice. If you'd prefer a SaaS experience, you can <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/elasticsearch/service\">sign up for a free trial of the Elasticsearch Service on Elastic Cloud</a>.&#160;&#160;\n</p><p>Of course, you can always download <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/start\">Elasticsearch and Kibana</a>, and provision your own clusters, or get the best of both worlds and choose <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/ece\">Elastic Cloud Enterprise</a>, which provides the SaaS experience of Elastic Cloud, but on your infrastructure.&#160;&#160;\n</p><p>The freedom to run how and where you choose gives you full control of where your data lives and its complete lifecycle.\n</p><h2>Elastic Observability</h2><p>Orchestration is one side of the coin. The other, of course, is observing the health and performance of those deployments. Our focus around Kubernetes and other CNCF projects is not solely based on orchestration of the Elastic Stack on Kubernetes, but digs deep into the observability of these systems and the workloads running on them as well. Automatic discovery of new or scaled containers or pods means that you don't need to update static configurations when your dynamic ecosystem changes, whether it be hour to hour, minute to minute, or second to second.&#160;&#160;\n</p><p>Elastic Observability lets you gather logs and metrics from your full application stack. With support for many CNCF projects — sandbox, graduated, and in between — gathering logs and metrics from your CNCF services and sending them to Elasticsearch is easy. Elastic also recently added support for resource quota metricsets, supporting all fields exposed by the <code>kube-state-metrics</code> agent, along with expanded support for ingesting Prometheus metrics by adding <code>remote_write</code> capabilities, bringing long-term, highly-available storage of high-cardinality Prometheus data.\n</p><p>Logs and metrics are a good start, but for full visibility you also need to see what the applications are doing both behind the scenes as well as how they behave from the outside — and that's where application performance monitoring (APM) and service monitoring come in.\n</p><p>Elastic APM provides developers with native language instrumentation agents for many common programming languages and frameworks, along with support for several other open source instrumentation services and frameworks. Elastic APM expanded open instrumentation support by implementing a <a href=\"https://elastic.zoom.us/j/94938108180?pwd=ZExXM01KbGR3WTJpeHpOMlQyaEhrUT09\">Jaeger intake</a>, allowing developers to send traces from the CNCF-graduated Jaeger project directly to Elastic APM. In addition, we recently created an <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/blog/elastic-apm-opentelemetry-integration\">OpenTelemetry collector</a> to allow gathering of OpenTelemetry traces, which joins the OpenTracing support that was added in 2018. &#160;\n</p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt494612fff7d43dfc/5f2449d07ca22b018aed5b77/apm-distributed-trace.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt494612fff7d43dfc\" alt=\"Elastic APM distributed tracing\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto;\">\n</p><p>APM shows what is going on inside your applications and services — where they are spending their time, what they are interacting with, and any errors that may be occurring — and combining APM with real user monitoring (<a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/blog/performing-real-user-monitoring-rum-with-elastic-apm\">RUM</a>) and service monitoring (Uptime and <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/beats/heartbeat\">Heartbeat</a>) allows you to accurately gauge your users' experience. Just because you've sent a response doesn't mean that the transaction is over, and just because it works for one user doesn't mean that it works for all of them.<br>\n</p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/bltea2565778bb04481/5f2449e37f3b7908db02fa47/uptime.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"bltea2565778bb04481\" alt=\"Service monitoring with Elastic Observability\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto;\">\n</p><p>Many organizations don't run entirely in one ecosystem, but rather across multiple public and private clouds with virtualization and bare metal sprinkled in: some workloads in an on-prem Kubernetes cluster, and others deployed across multiple cloud providers. When faced with a hybrid-cloud environment, many organizations end up with siloed monitoring tools, one per cloud provider. Elastic Observability allows you to \"monitor the monitors\", providing visibility across your hybrid infrastructure by consolidating logs and metrics from <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/beats/metricbeat/current/metricbeat-metricset-azure-monitor.html\">Azure Monitor</a>, Google Cloud Operations Suite (formerly known as <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/beats/metricbeat/current/metricbeat-module-googlecloud.html\">StackDriver</a>), and <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/beats/metricbeat/current/metricbeat-metricset-aws-cloudwatch.html\">AWS CloudWatch</a>, and interacting with that data alongside your Prometheus metrics, and all of your other logs and metrics, in one single datastore.\n</p><p>All of your logs, metrics, application traces, and availability data are stored in Elasticsearch indices, so you can mix and match, correlate and chart, and analyze and augment across different data streams.\n</p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt8021ef9e427afceb/5f244a0f7ca22b018aed5b7b/prometheus-alongside-logs-and-metrics.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt8021ef9e427afceb\" alt=\"Full observability with Elastic\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto;\">\n</p><h2>Teaming up with more use cases</h2><p>It's not just observability data that you can mix and match. Your observability data can be stored alongside your business KPIs as well — sales metrics, ads served, products shipped, you name it — giving you extra dimensions to slice and dice, and new ways to answer old questions. How much did that last outage cost? How did that last software change to our recommendation engine help sales? Or simple things, like determining the best time of day for an upgrade. With powerful visualizations and tools like <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/what-is/kibana-lens\">Kibana Lens</a> you can <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/webinars/visualizing-your-observability-data-through-the-kibana-lens\">answer those types of questions</a>.\n</p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/bltea29d5cdf26ac853/5f244a317ca22b018aed5b7f/annotations-and-kpis.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"bltea29d5cdf26ac853\" alt=\"Anomaly detection in observability data with Elastic\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto;\">\n</p><p>Observability is just one of the many use cases that the Elastic Stack supports. In the 1.2 release of ECK, <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/cloud-on-k8s/current/release-notes-1.2.0.html\">support was added</a> for orchestration of the enterprise search solution, along with a workflow for Beats, adding to the previously supported observability and security solutions.<br>\n</p><p>With Elastic Security, the ever-growing list of prebuilt detection rules lets you leverage observability data in your threat hunting efforts, strengthening the partnership between DevOps and SecOps, speeding up investigations without duplication of data and with no extra work or cost.\n</p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt7fd0478cf33065e7/5f244a4f9af97004e7f6159e/security-rules.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt7fd0478cf33065e7\" alt=\"Signal detection rules in Elastic Security\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto;\">\n</p><p>With built-in integrations to the free and open <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/kibana/current/alerting-getting-started.html\">Kibana Alerting and Actions</a>, you can set your rules and thresholds, then get notified via system log or index (or optionally via third party services like Slack, PagerDuty, or simple webhooks), letting you know when services are running outside of expectations or if you are in danger of breaching your SLAs.\n</p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/bltf45d0ce9ddbff446/5f244a6228ef1e01dfb4ef7f/metrics-alert.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"bltf45d0ce9ddbff446\" alt=\"Create alerts from anomalies\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto;\">\n</p><p>With the added advantage of automated anomaly detection that’s powered by machine learning, you can see at a glance which services are having trouble, or detect when something is behaving outside of normal parameters.\n</p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt4d05b1765c3c6b09/5f244a6f8e1e8d4046e9c466/anomaly-details.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt4d05b1765c3c6b09\" alt=\"Service maps in Elastic APM\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto;\">\n</p><h2>Get in touch</h2><p>If you're attending KubeCon Europe 2020 \"swing by\" our virtual booth and chat — we'd love to hear from you. 07:30:22","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 07:30:22","title":"Securely manage credentials while monitoring Kubernetes workloads with autodiscovery","link":"https://www.elastic.co/blog/securely-manage-credentials-monitoring-kubernetes-workloads-autodiscovery","description":"<p>In the world of containers and Kubernetes, <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/observability\">observability</a> is crucial. Cluster administrators need visibility into the infrastructure and cluster operators need to know the status of their workloads at any given time. And in both cases, they need observability into moving objects. </p> \n<p>This is where <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/beats/metricbeat\">Metricbeat</a> and its autodiscover feature do the hard part for you. Operators can set up fairly complex monitoring scenarios with a self-service approach following the principle of least privilege at the cluster level. </p> \n<p>The self-service approach is great, but what if the service I want to monitor is behind authentication? In this blog, we’ll take a look at how easy it is to configure observability for authenticated services with Elastic. </p> \n<h2>How autodiscover works</h2> \n<p>Applications running on containers and pods become moving targets to the monitoring system. Autodiscover tracks changes at the container and pod level and dynamically adjusts the monitoring configuration using two strategies: </p> \n<ol> \n <li><strong>Template-based autodiscover:</strong> By defining configuration templates, the autodiscover subsystem can use them to monitor services as they start running.</li> \n <li><strong>Hints-based autodiscover:</strong> The <code>hints</code> system looks for hints in Kubernetes pod annotations or Docker labels which have the prefix <code>co.elastic.metrics</code>. As soon as the container starts, Metricbeat will check if it contains any hints and launch the proper configuration for it. Hints tell Metricbeat how to get metrics for the given container.</li> \n</ol> \n<p>Let’s look at an example with <em>template-based autodiscover</em>. Here is a configuration template for automatically detecting Redis pods and starting to collect metrics using the respective Redis Metricbeat module: </p> \n<pre class=\"prettyprint\">metricbeat.autodiscover:\n  providers:\n    - type: kubernetes\n      templates:\n        - condition:\n            contains:\n              kubernetes.labels.app: \"redis\"\n          config:\n            - module: redis\n              metricsets: [\"info\"]\n              hosts: \"${data.host}:6379\"\n</pre> \n<p>Defining autodiscover templates at Metricbeat’s configuration level is a valid approach, but users must be granted the permission to globally configure Metricbeat. In addition, a restart of Metricbeat is required every time the autodiscover template is updated. With hints-based autodiscover, we let workload owners enable monitoring for their services on the fly, avoiding over-granted permissions without restarting the Beats. The following example configures the same Redis monitoring from above using hints: </p> \n<pre class=\"prettyprint\">metricbeat.autodiscover:\n  providers:\n    - type: kubernetes\n      hints.enabled: true\n</pre> \n<p>In this case, Redis pods need to be <a href=\"https://kubernetes.io/docs/concepts/overview/working-with-objects/annotations/\">annotated</a> accordingly: </p> \n<pre class=\"prettyprint\">apiVersion: v1\nkind: Pod\nmetadata:\n  name: annotations-demo\n  annotations:\n    co.elastic.metrics/module: redis\n    co.elastic.metrics/metricsets: info\n    co.elastic.metrics/hosts: '${data.host}:6379'\n</pre> \n<p><strong></strong> </p> \n<h2><strong>Using passwords with autodiscover</strong></h2> \n<p>In many cases, the target service requires password authentication in order to provide monitoring metrics. For instance, Redis can be configured to be password protected, meaning we have to provide a password to the Redis module in Metricbeat in order to collect metrics successfully. With template-based autodiscover, we would configure the template like this: </p> \n<pre class=\"prettyprint\">metricbeat.autodiscover:\n  providers:\n    - type: kubernetes\n      templates:\n        - condition:\n            contains:\n              kubernetes.labels.app: \"redis\"\n          config:\n            - module: redis\n              metricsets: [\"info\"]\n              hosts: \"${data.host}:6379\"\n              password: 'myred1sp@ss'\n</pre> \n<p>Similarly, with hints-based autodiscover, we would annotate the pod with a string containing the password: </p> \n<pre class=\"prettyprint\">apiVersion: v1\nkind: Pod\nmetadata:\n  name: annotations-demo\nannotations:\n  co.elastic.metrics/module: redis\n  co.elastic.metrics/metricsets: info\n  co.elastic.metrics/hosts: '${data.host}:6379'\n  co.elastic.metrics/password: 'myred1sp@ss'\n</pre> \n<p>While we can use these setups for development or testing purposes, storing passwords in plain text poses a serious risk and should be avoided. We could put the password in an environment variable and let Metricbeat interpolate the manifest, but this wouldn’t make our security posture any better. Let’s see a preferable alternative. </p> \n<strong> <h3>The Metricbeat keystore</h3></strong> \n<p>Metricbeat provides a feature called <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/beats/metricbeat/7.8/keystore.html\">keystore</a> that can be used to securely store sensitive information and reference it in the Metricbeat configuration as a secret. Let’s see how we can leverage this feature in our autodiscover template configurations. First, we create the keystore and add the password for Redis under the name <code>REDIS_PASSWORD</code>: </p> \n<pre class=\"prettyprint\">metricbeat keystore create\nmetricbeat keystore add REDIS_PASSWORD\n</pre> \n<p>And provide “myred1sp@ss” when prompted. </p> \n<p>Then the configuration template will be able to reference the string <code>REDIS_PASSWORD</code> to access the actual password: </p> \n<pre class=\"prettyprint\">metricbeat.autodiscover:\n  providers:\n    - type: kubernetes\n      templates:\n        - condition:\n            contains:\n              kubernetes.labels.app: \"redis\"\n          config:\n            - module: redis\n              metricsets: [\"info\"]\n              hosts: \"${data.host}:6379\"\n              password: \"${REDIS_PASSWORD}\"\n</pre> \n<p>And that’s all! We managed to avoid using plain text passwords or defining ENV variables, and instead we leveraged the Metricbeat keystore to securely store the password for the target Redis pod. </p> \n<p>Unfortunately, we can't use this strategy with hints-based autodiscovery — it wouldn’t be safe to expose the keystore to the hints mechanism because it could be a target for an attacker trying to exploit the whole keystore just using hints annotations. This is why we introduced a completely new Keystore, the <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/beats/metricbeat/7.9/configuration-autodiscover.html#_kubernetes_secrets\">Kubernetes secrets keystore</a>. </p> \n<strong> <h3>The Kubernetes Secrets Keystore</h3></strong> \n<p>With the Kubernetes Secrets Keystore we allow users to consume <a href=\"https://kubernetes.io/docs/concepts/configuration/secret/\">Kubernetes secrets</a> directly in autodiscover configuration. It can be used for both template-based and hints-based autodiscover. </p> \n<p>Users can refer a Kubernetes Secret with the following pattern: </p> \n<pre class=\"prettyprint\">${kubernetes.&lt;namespace&gt;.&lt;secret_name&gt;.&lt;value&gt;}\n</pre> \n<p>For instance, <code>${kubernetes.default.redisPass.value}</code> where <code>kubernetes.default.redisPass.value</code> specifies a key stored as a Kubernetes secret with the following: </p> \n<ol> \n <li>Kubernetes Namespace: <code>default</code></li> \n <li>Secret Name: <code>redisPass</code></li> \n <li>Secret Data Key: <code>value</code></li> \n</ol> \n<p>Note that pods can only consume secrets that belong to the same Kubernetes namespace. For example if our Redis pod is running in the <code>staging</code> namespace, it cannot access a secret created in the <code>testing</code> namespace. </p> \n<p>Let's see how we could leverage the new Keystore backend. First we need to create the secret: </p> \n<pre class=\"prettyprint\">cat &lt;&lt; EOF | kubectl apply -f -\napiVersion: v1\nkind: Secret\nmetadata:\n  name: redisPass\ntype: Opaque\ndata:\n  value: $(echo -n \"myred1sp@ss\" | base64)\nEOF\n</pre> \n<p>Now we can consume the <code>redisPass</code> secret from within our hint based autodiscover configuration: </p> \n<pre class=\"prettyprint\">apiVersion: v1\nkind: Pod\nmetadata:\n  name: annotations-demo\n  app: redis\nannotations:\n  co.elastic.metrics/module: redis\n  co.elastic.metrics/metricsets: info\n  co.elastic.metrics/hosts: '${data.host}:6379'\n  co.elastic.metrics/password: '${kubernetes.default.redisPass.value}'\n</pre> \n<p>Kubernetes secrets keystore also works with autodiscover templates and we can access Kuberentes secrets from our static configurations, too: </p> \n<pre class=\"prettyprint\">metricbeat.autodiscover:\n  providers:\n    - type: kubernetes\n      templates:\n        - condition:\n            contains:\n              kubernetes.labels.app: \"redis\"\n          config:\n            - module: redis\n              metricsets: [\"info\", \"keyspace\"]\n              hosts: \"${data.host}:6379\"\n              password: \"${kubernetes.default.redisPass.value}\"\n</pre> \n<strong> <h2>Conclusions</h2></strong> \n<p>If you find the idea of securing sensitive configurations in your Kubernetes environment of utmost importance, then you will love Metricbeat keystore and Kubernetes secrets keystore — they allow you to securely connect without leaking plain text passwords. </p> \n<p><a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/downloads\">Download Metricbeat</a> and start storing your passwords securely for your autodiscovered workloads without worrying about leaking sensitive data. And make sure your data stays safe by using Elasticsearch Service on <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/cloud\">Elastic Cloud</a>, where security comes standard. <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/elasticsearch/service\">Start a free trial today</a>. </p>If you have any questions, we are always happy to help on the Discuss \n<a href=\"https://discuss.elastic.co/\">forums</a>.","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Tue, 11 Aug 2020 16:00:00 +0000","feedId":7403,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"21151f59ca5645dd9c783654f2576e01","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"Christos Markou","publishedOrCreatedDate":1598311822917},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 07:30:22","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 07:30:22","title":"What's new in Logstash 7.9.0","link":"https://www.elastic.co/blog/whats-new-logstash-7-9-0","description":"<p>We are happy to announce the general availability of the Logstash 7.9.0 release. This is the latest stable release and is now available for <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/downloads/logstash\">download</a>! Please refer to the <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/logstash/7.9/releasenotes.html\">release notes</a> for the complete list of bug fixes and features.\n</p> <strong><h2>Faster pipeline startups and restarts&#160;</h2></strong><p>For some time now, we have heard clear feedback from our users that the compilation of complex Logstash pipelines may take extended periods of time to execute, effectively resulting in longer startup and restart times. In the Logstash 7.9.0 release, we are excited to introduce some substantial optimizations to pipeline compilation that result in over 9x faster pipeline startups and restarts. This should help&#160;streamline the pipeline development workflow and enhance overall operational excellence.\n</p><p>Through our tests with&#160;three different real world pipelines running&#160;8 workers each, we have seen pipeline compilation time reduced from&#160;up to 28 minutes down to just around a&#160;minute. For deeper technical details regarding these optimizations, please see <a href=\"https://github.com/elastic/logstash/pull/12060\">this PR</a>.&#160;Additionally, to assist with&#160;pipeline development and better understanding of the performance impact of compilation, Logstash now logs the time taken to compile each pipeline:\n</p><pre class=\"prettyprint\">[2020-08-12T14:10:29,388][INFO ][logstash.javapipeline    ][main] Pipeline Java execution initialization time {\"seconds\"=&#62;0.7}\n</pre> <strong><h2>Unlock more application search use cases<br>\n</h2></strong><p>With this Logstash release, we have taken the opportunity to completely revamp the <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/logstash/7.9/plugins-outputs-elastic_app_search.html\">Elastic App Search output plugin</a> with a more robust Ruby client library and greatly expanded integration testing coverage. This enables our users to unlock application search on their datasets that may currently reside in existing databases, raw/blob&#160;storage, and messaging queues. It also allows for an easy migration path from a custom Elasticsearch application search use case to <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/app-search/\">Elastic App Search</a>. This plugin has now graduated to a tier 1 plugin and is compatible with Elastic App Search 7.x\n</p><p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt770555c62fb6bdb0/5f3a235b9a5d3175f897b3aa/logstash-app-search-blog.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt770555c62fb6bdb0\" alt=\"logstash-app-search-blog.png\" \"=\"\">\n</p><strong><h2>Continued API key support</h2></strong><p>Following up on the Logstash 7.8.0 release where we added API key support for the Elasticsearch output plugin, Logstash 7.9.0 introduces additional API key support for the <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/logstash/7.9/plugins-inputs-elasticsearch.html#plugins-inputs-elasticsearch-api_key\">Elasticsearch input</a>, <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/logstash/7.9/plugins-filters-elasticsearch.html#plugins-filters-elasticsearch-api_key\">Elasticsearch filter</a>, <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/logstash/7.9/monitoring-internal-collection-legacy.html#monitoring-additional-settings-legacy\">Logstash monitoring</a>, and <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/logstash/7.9/configuring-centralized-pipelines.html#configuration-management-settings\">central pipeline management</a>.\n</p><p>Please check out <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/logstash/7.9/ls-security.html#ls-api-keys\">the documentation</a> for more information about using API keys with Logstash and Elasticsearch.\n</p> <strong><h2>Support for ARM/AARCH64</h2></strong><p>We always try our best to meet our users where they are, and with this release we continue down the path of making Logstash available on more platforms. The Logstash binaries are now&#160;tested&#160;to work against ARM/AARCH64 systems. This is only the initial step of the journey, and we are looking to offer Docker and other images in the near future as well. ARM/AARCH64 support&#160;is currently offered as <code>experimental</code> and therefore not yet recommended for production environments.\n</p><p>Please consult our <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/support/matrix\">support matrix</a> for the full list of operating systems that Logstash supports.\n</p> <strong><h2>Try it yourself!</h2></strong><p>Please <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/downloads/logstash\">download Logstash 7.9.0</a>, try it, and let us know what you think on Twitter (<a href=\"https://twitter.com/elastic\">@elastic</a>) or in our <a href=\"https://discuss.elastic.co/\">forums</a> and <a href=\"https://elasticstack.slack.com\">community Slack</a>. You can report any bugs or feature requests on the Logstash <a href=\"https://github.com/elastic/logstash/issues\">Github issues page</a> or within the <a href=\"https://github.com/logstash-plugins\">respective plugin repositories</a>.\n</p>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Wed, 19 Aug 2020 16:01:01 +0000","feedId":7403,"bgimg":"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt770555c62fb6bdb0/5f3a235b9a5d3175f897b3aa/logstash-app-search-blog.png","linkMd5":"331171483c5ef520d56d7d3a09c1bd8d","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn16@2020_3/2020/08/24/23-30-23-344_bec289a8c3938cb3.webp","destWidth":1674,"destHeight":650,"sourceBytes":72794,"destBytes":32238,"author":"Alvin Chen","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt770555c62fb6bdb0/5f3a235b9a5d3175f897b3aa/logstash-app-search-blog.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn16@2020_3/2020/08/24/23-30-23-344_bec289a8c3938cb3.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598311822917},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 07:30:22","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 07:30:22","title":"Elastic Security 7.9 adds anti-malware, prebuilt cloud protections, and more","link":"https://www.elastic.co/blog/whats-new-elastic-security-7-9-0-free-endpoint-security","description":"<p>Security teams frequently use Elastic Security to collect and analyze endpoint data, and the need to enhance visibility as the virtual workforce grows is making this use case even more common. The solution excels with use cases requiring data at scale — delivered in any format, at nearly any volume and velocity. Just deploy Beats across your endpoints, and voilà, you’re collecting far and wide.</p>\n<p>This leads us to ask: if you’ve deployed an agent to collect endpoint data, why not use that same agent to prevent malware? </p>\n<p>Elastic Security 7.9 delivers the first major milestone toward comprehensive endpoint security integrated directly into the Elastic Stack by introducing signatureless malware prevention and kernel-level data collection on the new <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/blog/introducing-elastic-agent-and-ingest-manager\">Elastic Agent</a>, our “one Beat to rule them all.” The Elastic Agent also brings together logging, metric collection, and more. These capabilities are available now in our free distribution tier, with additional endpoint protection features planned for later releases.</p>\n<p>Elastic Security 7.9 has even more in store. With data, applications, and users quickly moving to the cloud, SecOps teams must still maintain visibility across their hybrid environment. To address this, 7.9 introduces new detections that enable SecOps to preserve situational awareness without hindering the speed or agility of IT and dev teams performing migration. Community-driven workflow refinements streamline prevention, detection, and response. And a trove of newly supported data sources equip practitioners to stop threats, wherever they’re revealed.</p>\n<p>Let’s dig in.</p>\n<h2 id=\"freemalwarepreventionanddeependpointvisibility\">Free malware prevention and deep endpoint visibility</h2>\n<h3 id=\"antimalwareinoneclickbeta\">Anti-malware in one click (beta)</h3>\n<p>Malware prevention is now built into Elastic Agent, adding an important layer of protection to endpoint data collection. This new capability is available in our free tier to users and organizations everywhere. This advances our goal of building threat prevention directly into Elastic Agent, with many more advanced endpoint security capabilities to come.</p>\n<p>Signatureless malware prevention puts an immediate stop to malicious executables on Windows and macOS endpoints. In its latest round of testing, <a href=\"https://www.av-comparatives.org/tests/business-security-test-2020-march-june/\">AV-Comparatives</a> found that the Elastic Security machine learning model <a href=\"https://www.av-comparatives.org/tests/business-security-test-2020-march-june/\">stops 99.6% of malware</a> and triggers zero false positives on common business software. Activating anti-malware takes just one click in Kibana.\n<img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt01c1aa5d3d50b50a/5f3c4f9580360a1fd38bc4aa/screen-recording-security-endpoint-admin-activate.gif\" alt=\"One click to protect from malware with Elastic Agent\" /></p>\n<h3 id=\"kernelleveldatacollectionbeta\">Kernel-level data collection (beta)</h3>\n<p>Elastic Security facilitates high-fidelity alerting by collecting endpoint data for centralized analysis. MITRE ATT&#38;CK©-aligned detections from the Elastic Security Research Team leverage information all the way down to the kernel, powering detection rules and machine learning jobs, while minimizing false positives. The solution offers broad support, enabling collection of essential data, whatever the underlying OS:</p>\n<ul>\n<li>Windows datasets: Process, Network, File, DNS, Registry, DLL and Driver Loads, Security</li>\n<li>macOS datasets: Process, File, Network</li>\n<li>Linux datasets: Process, File, Network</li>\n</ul>\n<p>Centralizing endpoint data from across your environment and adding further context enables organizations to accelerate threat hunting, alert triage, and incident response. With the speed and scale of Elastic Security, you can quickly analyze detailed endpoint data, examine and contextualize information with interactive visualizations, and integrate response workflows with external orchestration and ticketing tools, like SOAR platforms.</p>\n<h2 id=\"prebuiltcloudprotectionsandexpandeddetectionoptions\">Prebuilt cloud protections and expanded detection options</h2>\n<h3 id=\"cloudsecurityposturemonitoringprotectionsrulesbetamljobsga\">Cloud security posture monitoring protections (rules beta / ML jobs GA)</h3>\n<p><a href=\"https://enterprise.verizon.com/resources/reports/dbir/\">Verizon’s 2020 DBIR</a> estimates that configuration errors are causal events in 22% of data breaches. The stakes grow as organizations transition to the cloud, but 99% of configuration errors are still being missed. The security content shared in our latest release reduces risk by enabling security teams to keep a continuous eye on misconfigured assets, permissions, and user activity.\n<img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt4c2f91974908d709/5f3c4fc85c201f2fe7f99580/screen-recording-security-endpoint-detection-admin.gif\" alt=\"Activating a detection rule analyzing endpoint data from Elastic Agent\" />\nElastic Security 7.9 safeguards cloud infrastructure and applications with new machine learning jobs and threat detection rules, helping prevent damage and loss. Like other Elastic-developed material, this new security content is aligned with the MITRE ATT&#38;CK framework. It enables continuous analysis of data from both AWS CloudTrail and Okta to support cloud infrastructure monitoring and identity and access monitoring.</p>\n<p>Have other use cases in mind? Explore the <a href=\"https://github.com/elastic/detection-rules\">Detection Rules repo</a>, which <a href=\"https://www.elastic.co/blog/elastic-security-opens-public-detection-rules-repo\">we recently opened</a> to facilitate collaboration between the Elastic Security Research Team and community members.</p>\n<h3 id=\"thresholdbasedalertingbeta\">Threshold-based alerting (beta)</h3>\n<p>Threshold-based rules, a new detection type, provides a new way to detect threats with Elastic Security, firing when more than a user-specified number of events for a given entity (e.g., an endpoint or user) meet a particular set of criteria. This enables environment-specific tuning of rules for use cases like detecting brute force attacks and spotting log sources that have stopped reporting.\n<img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt493178459199d8b5/5f3c565562013530f82eb44b/screenshot-security-detecton-new-threshold.png\" alt=\"enter image description here\" /></p>\n<h3 id=\"buildingblockruledesignationbeta\">Building block rule designation (beta)</h3>\n<p>Rules specified as “building blocks” remove generated alerts from analyst workflows, allowing the creation of inputs to trigger high-fidelity alerts without causing increased noise for analysts. You could, for example, use two building block rules — one configured to alert on abnormally high web server logins and the other configured to spot out-of-schedule maintenance activity on a database server — as inputs for a rule generating alerts that are more likely to merit analyst attention.</p>\n<h2 id=\"streamlineanalystworkflowswithcommunitydrivenrefinements\">Streamline analyst workflows with community-driven refinements</h2>\n<p>Elastic strives to deliver the intuitive, efficient, and delightful user experiences that security practitioners deserve. This takes rigor, but it’s worth it, because security teams spend countless hours in front of screens in pursuit of their mission to protect their customers, colleagues, and organizations.</p>\n<p>Our UX team works hard to meet a lofty goal: finding the balance between simplicity for new users and flexibility for advanced users. They transform community feedback into operational efficiency, so thank you for your direct input into many of these enhancements to Elastic Security.</p>\n<h3 id=\"interactiveprocesstreevisualizationbeta\">Interactive process tree visualization (beta)</h3>\n<p>A new interactive visualization of endpoint-based activity in Elastic Security 7.9 streamlines analyst triage, hunt, investigation, and response workflows. It equips analysts to spot potential adversary behaviors and attack progression (e.g., XLS file opened, process spawns, new outbound connection established). An option to attach a visualization (and associated data) to a new or existing case further streamlines workflows.</p>\n<p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blta55304129dbc1b94/5f3c53095c201f2fe7f99590/screenshot-security-host-process-tree-visualization.png\" alt=\"Viewing processes on an interactive visualization\" /></p>\n<p>Endpoint context is critical to SecOps visibility, but too often, performance limitations or unmanageable costs prevent the centralized analysis of this data. This integration between the SIEM and endpoint security capabilities of Elastic Security enhances environmental visibility, reduces analyst guesswork, and increases team efficiency.</p>\n<h3 id=\"investigationguidesforprebuiltdetectionrulesbeta\">Investigation guides for prebuilt detection rules (beta)</h3>\n<p>All detection rules introduced in version 7.9 feature richly formatted investigation guides that suggest questions, actions, and next steps for analysts to consider when investigating a detection alert. Customizing an investigation guide for your environment or implementing one for a new rule is as simple as updating a markdown field.\n<img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/bltf12741cddacf6c69/5f3c611bd5b383280ff0ee17/screenshot-security-detection-investigation-guide-anon.png\" alt=\"Investigation guide associated with a prebuilt detection rule\" /></p>\n<h3 id=\"customizableinvestigationtimelinetemplatesbeta\">Customizable investigation timeline templates (beta)</h3>\n<p>Elastic Security 7.9 enables users to customize how data is filtered and presented on the investigation timeline through “timeline templates” associated with individual detection rules. Users can duplicate, customize, create, and share new timeline templates, which specify both the filter and columns (which fields and in what order). These curated views enable analysts to proceed directly from initial review of a detection alert to investigation, without pausing to re-configure the filter or viewable fields. Elastic security researchers align timeline templates with the investigation guides presented on new detection alerts, accelerating threat hunting and investigation.</p>\n<h3 id=\"unifiedalertexceptionsworkflowbeta\">Unified alert exceptions workflow (beta)</h3>\n<p>Elastic Security 7.9 helps analysts reduce false positives, a significant and longstanding analyst frustration with common security technologies. The release makes it easy to create rule exceptions that prevent an alert from being triggered when specific conditions are met. Exceptions can be added individually or with a list, and detection rules and agent configurations can be updated in parallel, reducing the overhead associated with minimizing false positives.\n<img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt89c231433d961fe4/5f3c5145752d292b6ca4e51a/screenshot-security-exception-add.png\" alt=\"Adding an exception to an Elastic Security detection rule\" /></p>\n<h3 id=\"streamlinedcaseworkflowswithibmsecurityresilientintegrationbeta\">Streamlined case workflows with IBM Security Resilient integration (beta)</h3>\n<p>Our new IBM Security Resilient integration enables security teams to readily integrate their security operations with the widely-deployed security incident response platform. Perform an investigation with Elastic Security, add investigation timelines and comments, and easily escalate by opening or updating a case within IBM Security Resilient — with just a click.</p>\n<h3 id=\"furtheranalystinterfaceenhancementsga\">Further analyst interface enhancements (GA)</h3>\n<p>Elastic Security 7.9 delivers numerous other big and small UI enhancements that we expect to prove indispensable: full-screen mode, infinite scroll, custom IP reputation lookup links, and more. Many of these enhancements began as community requests, and together in version 7.9, they contribute to a more refined user experience.</p>\n<h2 id=\"simplifydataingestionwithexpandedoutoftheboxdatacollectionandpreparation\">Simplify data ingestion with expanded out-of-the-box data collection and preparation</h2>\n<h3 id=\"newdataintegrationsgaexceptwherenoted\">New data integrations (GA, except where noted)</h3>\n<p>Elastic Security provides a curated experience for onboarding data, enabling SecOps, SecDevOps, and other teams to focus on the task at hand and not worry about wrangling data or chasing down data sources needed to support additional use cases. Version 7.9 adds integrations with new sources of endpoint and cloud data, including Microsoft Defender ATP, Windows PowerShell, Sophos XG firewalls, and beta support for Google G Suite (e.g., logins, admin activity, Drive activity).</p>\n<p><img src=\"https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/bltced0954e6dff60a5/5f3c572e5c201f2fe7f9959c/screenshot-hosts-events-graph.png\"
